Nikah — Marriage
intermediate
10 min
Scholarly Differences on Women Contracting Marriage: Intra-Hanafi and Inter-Madhhab Disputes
Detailing the intra-Hanafi and inter-madhhab differences on the requirement of a guardian for a woman's marriage.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three Hanafi positions and the Shafi'i/Maliki position on the role of a guardian in a woman's marriage, and explain the practical consequences of each view.

Core Idea
The Hanafi school has three positions: valid, invalid, or suspended; the mufta bihi is validity without guardian for adult women.

While the apparent narration of the Hanafi school permits a woman to marry without a guardian, other narrations within the school and other schools differ. This lesson unpacks the differences: Abu Yusuf's second narration requiring a guardian, Muhammad's suspended view, and the Shafi'i/Maliki insistence on a guardian for validity. Understanding these ikhtilāfāt is crucial for a mufti when answering questions.

Guided Path
1
List the three Hanafi positions
From the text: Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf (zahir) → valid. Abu Yusuf (other narration) → invalid without wali. Muhammad → suspended (mawquf) until wali approves.
Note the authority: zahir al-riwayah is the strongest.
2
Identify the Shafi'i/Maliki position
The text says Malik and Shafi'i hold that marriage is not contracted by women's expression. They require a guardian for validity.
Their reasoning: marriage is for certain purposes, and delegating it to women is detrimental.
3
Analyze the basis for Muhammad's mawquf view
Muhammad says the defect (lack of wali) is removed by the guardian's subsequent permission (ijāzah). Thus the contract is valid but without effect until consent.
This intermediate view tries to reconcile the woman's right with the need for familial oversight.
4
Determine the mufta bihi
The text indicates that the position of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf in zahir al-riwayah is the relied-upon view. Muhammad's view is also considered, but the stronger position is immediate validity.
Later Hanafi works (like al-Hidayah) adopt the view that the marriage is valid and not suspended.

Source Text

ترجمه. .... یہ باب اولیاء اور اکفاء کے بیان میں ہے

Chapter on Guardians and Suitability. Translation: This chapter is about guardians and suitability. Explanation: 'Awliya' is plural of 'wali', derived from 'wilayah', and wilayah means executing command over others, i.e., enforcing a ruling on another. 'Akfa' is plural of 'kufu', and kufu means peer or equal. A free, sane, adult woman, whether virgin or non-virgin, with her consent, her marriage is valid without a guardian. And the marriage of a free, sane, adult woman is contracted with her consent, even if no guardian contracts it, whether she is a virgin or non-virgin, according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf in the apparent narration. And from Abu Yusuf it is narrated that it is not contracted except with a guardian. And according to Muhammad, it is contracted but suspended (mawquf). And Malik and Shafi'i said: marriage is not contracted by the expression of women, because marriage is intended for its purposes and delegating to them is detrimental to it, except that Muhammad says the defect is removed by the guardian's permission. And the basis of permissibility is that she is acting in her pure right and she is of its people because...
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage introduces Chapter on Guardians and Suitability in marriage. The text defines key terms: 'awliyā' (pl. of wali, from wilāyah meaning authority over others) and 'akfā' (pl. of kufu, meaning equal or match). It then states the ruling: a free, sane, adult woman can marry herself without a guardian, with her consent, whether she is a virgin or previously married. This is the position of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf in the apparent narration (zāhir al-riwāyah). There is a weaker narration from Abu Yusuf that a guardian is required. Muhammad says the marriage is suspended (mawqūf) until the guardian permits. Imam Malik and Imam Shafi'i hold that women cannot contract their own marriage. The text gives a brief reason: marriage is for specific purposes, and delegating it to women is detrimental. Muhammad's view is that the guardian's permission can cure the defect. The permissibility is based on the woman acting in her own right (her pure right), and she is qualified (min ahlihi) to do so. The passage cuts off mid-sentence, so the full argument is not provided here.

Key Terms
موقوف (mawquf): Suspended; a marriage that is valid but awaits approval (ijāzah) from the guardian to become fully effective.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the ruling of Imam Muhammad (al-Shaybani) on a woman's marriage without a guardian?
A) The marriage is valid and effective immediately.
B) The marriage is invalid and must be dissolved.
C) The marriage is suspended (mawquf) until the guardian permits it.
D) The marriage is only valid if the woman is a non-virgin.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school has multiple positions on the guardian requirement; the mufta bihi is that an adult sane woman's marriage without a guardian is valid and immediately effective.

Nikah — Marriage
intermediate
8 min
Marriage Without a Guardian: The Hanafi Position and Intra-Madhhab Dispute
Detailed exposition of intra-Hanafi and inter-madhhab dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position (zahir al-riwayah) on a free adult woman contracting her own marriage, identify the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf, and explain the Hanafi proof against the Shafi'i/Maliki objection.

Core Idea
A free adult woman's marriage is valid without a guardian in the Hanafi school, based on her legal capacity.

This lesson addresses a classic point of ikhtilaf: whether a free, sane, adult woman can marry herself without a guardian. While the Shafi'i and Maliki schools require a guardian, the Hanafi school in its authoritative narrations permits it. We will examine the Hanafi proof, the intra-madhhab differences among Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad, and the practical implications for a mufti.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
Determine the ruling (sahih) from the zahir al-riwayah: the marriage is valid with her consent even without a guardian, whether virgin or previously married. This is the mufta bihi position.
Focus on Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf in zahir al-riwayah.
2
Map the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
List the three Hanafi positions: valid (Abu Hanifa & Abu Yusuf zahir), invalid without guardian (Abu Yusuf non-zahir), and suspended on guardian's permission (Muhammad). Note the authority weight: zahir al-riwayah is preferred.
Also note the report that Muhammad retracted to the view of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf.
3
Analyse the Hanafi proof
Examine the basis: the woman disposes in her pure right (the dower is hers), she is qualified (sane and discerning), so she has the capacity to choose a spouse and dispose of property. The guardian is only involved to avoid shamelessness.
This is a dalil from capacity and right.
4
Evaluate the opposing argument
Shafi'i/Maliki argue that marriage has purposes (maqasid) that women are deficient in intellect to handle, so entrusting it to them is detrimental. Note Muhammad's response that the guardian's permission removes the detriment.
The Hanafi response also emphasizes the woman's full right and capacity.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical difference: Under the Hanafi mufta bihi, a woman's self-contracted marriage is valid. Under Shafi'i/Maliki, it is invalid. Additionally, in the Hanafi school, the guardian retains the right to object in case of kufu (inequality), but the marriage itself is valid.
This impacts how a mufti advises on marriage contracts.

Source Text

کو۔ وينعقد نكاح الحرة العاقلة البالغة برضائها وان لم يعقد عليها ولى بكرا كانت اوثيبا عند ابي حنيفة وابي يوسف في ظاهر الرواية وعن ابى يوسف انه لا ينعقد الابولى وعند محمد ينعقد موقوفا وقال مالک والشافعي ہیں۔ اور ان مقاصد نکاح کو عورتوں کے سپرد کرنا ان میں خلل ڈالنا ہے مگر یہ کہ امام محمد فرماتے ہیں کہ نہیں ولی کی اجازت سے رفع ہو جائے گا۔ اور جواز کی وجہ یہ ہے کہ اس عورت نے خالص اپنے حق میں تصرف کیا الروایت کی روایت کے مطابق شیخین کے نزدیک منعقد ہو سکتا ہے اور ابو یوسف سے غیر ظاہر الروایت کی روایت میں ایک روایت ہے کہ بغیر ولی کے نکاح منعقد نہیں ہوگا اور امام محمد کے نزدیک موقوف ہو کر منعقد ہو گا اگر ولی اجازت دیدیگا تو نافذ ہوگا ورنہ نہیں۔ امام مالک اور امام فر فر ماتے ہیں کہ عورتوں کے الفاظ سے بالکل منعقد نہیں ہوگا۔ امام مالک اور شافعی کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ نکاح سے مقاصد نکاح مقصود ہوتے ہیں ان مقاصد کو عورتوں کے سپر دکرنے میں ان میں خلل ڈالنا ہے اس لئے کہ عورتیں ناقصات عقل ہوتی ہیں۔ اس واسطے عورتوں کو نکاح کا اختیار نہیں دیں گے۔ امام محمد نے دلیل میں کہا کہ بات تو صحیح ہے لیکن ولی کے اجازت دینے سے یہ خلل رفع ہو جائے گا۔ کیونکہ ولی اگر مناسب سمجھے تو اجازت دے گا اور اگر مناسب نہیں سمجھے گا تو اجازت نہیں دے گا۔ وجہ جواز یہ ہے کہ اس عورت نے خالص اپنے حق میں تصرف کیا ہے چنانچہ بضع کا بدل اسی کے لئے ہوگا اور یہ عورت تصرف کی اہل بھی ہے اس لئے کہ عقل ممیز رکھتی ہے۔ یہی وجہ ہے کہ مال میں تصرف کا اختیار اس عورت کو حاصل ہو گا۔ ہاں اگر کوئی اشکال کرے کہ جب عورت با اختیار ہے تو پھر نکاح کا مطالبہ ولی سے کیوں کیا جاتا ہے عورتوں سے کیوں نہیں کیا جاتا۔ اس کا جواب یہ ہے کہ ایسا کرنے سے عورتوں کی طرف بے حیائی کو منسوب کیا جائیگا۔ کیونکہ عورتیں مردوں کی مجالس کی طرف نکلنے میں حیا محسوس کریں گی۔ اگر ایسا کریں گی تو لوگ ان کو بے حیا قرار دیں گے۔ پھر ظاہر الروایت میں کفو اور غیر کفو میں کوئی فرق نہیں۔ یعنی اگر حرہ عاقلہ بالغہ نے اپنا نکاح خود کر لیا کفو میں یا بغیر کفو کے تو نکاح جائز ہے لیکن غیر کفو کی صورت میں ولی کو حق اعتراض حاصل رہے گا۔ اپنے اوپر سے ضرر عار کو دفع کرنے کے لئے۔ اور ابوحنیفہ اور ابو یوسف سے نوادر میں ایک روایت ہے کہ غیر کفو میں نکاح جائز ہے۔
Chapter on Guardians and Equals. Explanation: 'Awliya' is plural of 'wali', derived from 'wilayah', which means implementing orders over others, i.e., enforcing judgment over others. 'Akfa'' is plural of 'kufu', which means peer and equal. And the marriage of a free, sane, adult woman is contracted with her consent even if a guardian did not contract it for her, whether she is a virgin or a previously married woman, according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf in the apparent narration. And from Abu Yusuf (in a non-apparent narration) that it is not contracted except with a guardian. And according to Muhammad, it is contracted as suspended (mawquf). Malik and al-Shafi'i said: The marriage is not contracted by the words of women, because marriage is intended for its purposes, and entrusting it to women is detrimental to them, except that Muhammad says the detriment is removed by the guardian's permission. And the basis of permissibility is that she disposed in her pure right, and she is qualified for it because she is sane and discerning, so she has the right to dispose of property and choose husbands. The guardian is only demanded to marry her off so that she is not attributed to shamelessness. Then in the apparent narration, there is no difference between an equal and a non-equal, but the guardian has the right to object in the case of a non-equal. And from Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf (in a non-apparent narration) that it is not permissible with a non-equal, because many cases do not go to court. And it is narrated that Muhammad retracted to their saying. Translation and explanation: The marriage of a free, sane, adult woman is contracted with her consent even if the guardian did not contract it, whether virgin or previously married, according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf in the apparent narration. And from Abu Yusuf (in a non-apparent narration) it is narrated that it will not be contracted without a guardian. And according to Imam Muhammad, it will be contracted as suspended, because the purposes of marriage are intended from marriage, and entrusting these purposes to women causes detriment, except that Imam Muhammad says the detriment is removed by the guardian's permission. The basis of permissibility is that this woman disposed in her pure right, and she is qualified for disposition because she is sane and discerning, and for this reason she has the right to dispose of property and choose husbands. The guardian is demanded to marry her so that she is not attributed to shamelessness. Then in the apparent narration, there is no difference between equal and non-equal, but the guardian has the right to object in non-equal. And from Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf it is narrated that it is not permissible in non-equal, because many cases do not go to court. And it is narrated that Imam Muhammad retracted to the saying of the two shaykhs. Explanation: The issue is whether the marriage of a free, sane, adult woman is contracted without a guardian with her consent. According to the apparent narration of the two shaykhs, it can be contracted. And from Abu Yusuf, in a non-apparent narration, there is a narration that it will not be contracted without a guardian. According to Imam Muhammad, it will be contracted as suspended; if the guardian permits, it becomes effective, otherwise not. Imam Malik and Imam al-Shafi'i say it will not be contracted at all by the words of women. Their proof is that marriage is intended for purposes, and entrusting these purposes to women causes detriment because women are deficient in intellect, so women are not given the authority of marriage. Imam Muhammad responded that what they said is correct, but the detriment is removed by the guardian's permission, because the guardian will permit if he deems it appropriate, otherwise not. The basis of permissibility is that this woman disposed in her pure right, so the dower is for her, and she is qualified for disposition because she possesses discerning intellect. For this reason, she has the right to dispose of property. If one objects: when the woman has authority, why is the guardian demanded to marry her and not the woman? The answer is that doing so would attribute shamelessness to women because women feel shy to go out to men's gatherings; if they did so, people would consider them shameless. Then in the apparent narration, there is no difference between equal and non-equal, meaning if a free, sane, adult woman contracts her marriage herself, whether equal or not, the marriage is valid, but in the case of non-equal, the guardian retains the right to object to remove harm and disgrace from himself. And from Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf there is a narration in the Nawadir that marriage with a non-equal is permissible.
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi and inter-madhhab dispute regarding the marriage of a free, sane, adult woman without a guardian. The author first defines the terms 'wali' (guardian) and 'kufu' (equality). The core ruling is stated: according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf in the zahir al-riwayah, such a marriage is valid with her consent even without a guardian, whether she is a virgin or previously married. Then three other positions are noted: (1) A non-zahir al-riwayah report from Abu Yusuf that it is invalid without a guardian; (2) Imam Muhammad's view that it is suspended (mawquf) upon the guardian's permission; (3) The Shafi'i/Maliki view that a woman's own utterance cannot contract marriage at all. The passage then provides proofs: For permissibility, the woman has full right over her dower and is qualified to dispose of her property, so she can choose a husband. The guardian is only involved to avoid shamelessness. Regarding kufu, in the zahir al-riwayah, the marriage is valid even if there is no equality, but the guardian can object if there is lack of equality. In a nawadir narration from Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, marriage with a non-equal is not valid because many cases do not go to court. It is reported that Imam Muhammad retracted to the view of the two shaykhs. The commentary explains that the woman's authority is derived from her legal capacity, and the guardian's objection right is to protect the family from dishonor. The passage emphasizes that in the apparent narration, the woman's marriage is valid regardless of kufu, but the guardian has standing to challenge it if there is inequality.

Key Terms
ولی (wali): A guardian who has authority to contract marriage for a woman; defined as 'تنفيذ الامر على الغير' (implementing orders over others).
کفو (kufu): Equality in status (lineage, wealth, etc.) between spouses.
ظاهر الرواية (zahir al-riwayah): The well-known and authoritative narrations from the Hanafi school, especially from the six books of Imam Muhammad.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the zahir al-riwayah, what is the ruling if a free adult woman marries a non-kufu man without a guardian?
A) The marriage is void.
B) The marriage is valid but the guardian can object.
C) The marriage is suspended.
D) The marriage is valid only if the guardian consents.

Key Takeaway
The mufta bihi Hanafi position is that a free, sane, adult woman's marriage is valid without a guardian; the guardian's role is limited to objecting in cases of inequality, not to invalidate the contract.

Nikah — Marriage
advanced
10 min
Marriage of a Free Adult Woman Without a Guardian
Ruling with intra-Hanafi and inter-madhhab disputes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on the validity of marriage without a guardian for a free adult woman, and evaluate the Shafi'i/Maliki counter-argument.

Core Idea
A free adult woman's marriage without a guardian is valid in Hanafi law, but guardians may object if the husband is incompatible.

This lesson unpacks a central dispute in Islamic marriage law: can a woman contract her own marriage? The Hanafi school, uniquely, affirms her capacity, while other schools require a guardian. We'll explore the proofs, the internal Hanafi disagreement, and the practical outcome.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Clearly articulate the Hanafi ruling from Zahir al-Riwayah: the marriage of a free, adult, sane woman is valid without a guardian, regardless of compatibility, but the guardian may object in non-compatibility.
This is the mufta bihi (operative) position.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Name the opposing schools: Imam Malik and Imam Shafi'i, who hold that such a marriage is invalid without a guardian. Also note the minority Hanafi narration from Abu Yusuf (non-Zahir) and Imam Muhammad's initial suspension view.
Inter-madhhab and intra-Hanafi disputes overlap here.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Examine the proof of Malik and Shafi'i: women are deficient in intellect and lack experience in marriage, so entrusting them with the contract corrupts the objectives of nikah.
This is an argument from qiyas (analogy with minors).
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
Understand the Hanafi rebuttal: the woman's legal capacity (ʿāqila mumayyiza) gives her full authority over her own property and person; marriage is a disposal in her own right. The guardian's role is social, not legal.
Imam Muhammad initially argued the defect is removed by guardian's permission, but later reverted to the direct validity view.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Identify the practical consequence: In Hanafi fiqh, a woman can marry without a guardian (though guardian can object in non-kufu); in Shafi'i/Maliki, such marriage is invalid.
This has real-world implications for community practice and personal law.

منعقد ہو جائیگا اور اجازت ولی پر موقوف بھی نہیں رہے گا۔
A sane discerning woman, and therefore she has disposal over wealth and choice of husbands. The guardian is asked to marry her so that she is not attributed with shamelessness. Then in the apparent narration, there is no difference between compatible and non-compatible, but the guardian has the right to object in non-compatible. And from Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf that it is not permissible in non-compatible, because many occurrences are not raised. And it is narrated that Muhammad returned to their statement. Translation: The marriage of a free adult woman is concluded with her consent, even if the guardian did not contract it, whether she is a virgin or a widow. According to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf in the apparent narration. And from Abu Yusuf (in non-apparent narration) it is narrated that it will not be concluded without a guardian. And according to Imam Muhammad, it will be concluded suspended. Because the objectives of marriage are intended, and entrusting these objectives to women introduces defect, except that Imam Muhammad says that it will be removed by the guardian's permission. And the reason for permissibility is that the woman disposed in her own right, and she is capable of disposal because she is sane and discerning, and therefore she has authority over wealth and choice of husbands, and the guardian is asked to marry her so that she is not attributed with shamelessness. Then in the apparent narration, there is no difference between compatible and non-compatible, but the guardian has the right to object in non-compatible. And from Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf it is narrated that it is not permissible in non-compatible, because many occurrences are not raised, and it is narrated that Imam Muhammad returned to the statement of the two. Explanation: A free, sane, adult woman's marriage with her consent without a guardian: can it be concluded or not? According to the apparent narration, by the two it can be concluded. And from Abu Yusuf in a non-apparent narration, it will not be concluded without a guardian. And according to Imam Muhammad, it will be concluded suspended: if the guardian permits, it will be effective; otherwise not. Imam Malik and Imam Shafi'i say that it will not be concluded absolutely by the words of women. The proof of Imam Malik and Shafi'i is that the objectives of marriage are intended; entrusting these objectives to women introduces defect because women are deficient in intellect; therefore women will not be given authority over marriage. Imam Muhammad said in proof: The statement is correct, but this defect is removed by the guardian's permission, because if the guardian deems it appropriate he will permit, and if not he will not permit. The reason for permissibility is that the woman disposed in her own right, so the exchange for the vulva is for her, and she is capable of disposal because she possesses discerning intellect; therefore she has authority over wealth. If someone objects: When the woman is authorized, why is the guardian asked for marriage and not the women? The answer is: Doing so would attribute shamelessness to women, because women would feel shy in going out to men's gatherings; if they do so, people would consider them shameless. Then in the apparent narration, there is no difference between compatible and non-compatible, i.e., if a free adult woman contracts her own marriage with a compatible or non-compatible man, the marriage is valid, but in case of non-compatibility, the guardian has the right to object, to remove harm and disgrace from himself. And from Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf in the rare narrations there is a narration that marriage in non-compatibility is not permissible, because many occurrences happen that after the marriage takes place, no one is able to repel it; not every guardian can present a case, nor is every judge just. Therefore caution lies in closing the door of marriage without guardian in non-compatibility. And it was narrated that Imam Muhammad reverted to the statement of the two, which is the apparent narration, i.e., according to Imam Muhammad as well, marriage will be concluded without a guardian and will not be suspended on his permission.
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage discusses the validity of marriage contracted by a free, adult, sane woman without her guardian's involvement. It is an intra-Hanafi and inter-madhhab dispute. The author first presents the Hanafi position from Zahir al-Riwayah (the relied-upon narrations) that such a marriage is valid with her consent, regardless of whether she is a virgin or a widow. This is based on her legal capacity: she is discerning (ʿāqila mumayyiza) and has full authority over her property, and marriage is a disposal of her own right (the dower is for her). The guardian's role is only to avoid imputation of shamelessness; he is asked to perform the marriage to protect her reputation. Then the passage discusses the condition of compatibility (kafāʾa): in Zahir al-Riwayah, there is no difference between a compatible and non-compatible match—the marriage is valid in either case, but the guardian has a right to object in non-compatibility to ward off harm. However, there is a narration from Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf (in non-apparent narrations) that marriage in non-compatibility is invalid because many cases go unraised (i.e., guardians cannot always object effectively). Imam Muhammad initially held that the marriage is suspended (mawqūf) on the guardian's permission, but later reverted to the position of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, making the marriage valid outright. The passage then contrasts the Hanafi view with that of Imam Malik and Imam Shafiʿi, who hold that a woman's own marriage contract is entirely invalid. Their proof: women are deficient in intellect and unaware of the objectives of marriage, so entrusting them with the contract introduces corruption. The Hanafi response (from Imam Muhammad) acknowledges the potential defect but states that it is removed by the guardian's permission (in the suspension view) or that the woman's capacity suffices (in the final view). The text also answers an objection: if the woman is competent, why is the guardian asked? The response: it is to protect her from being seen as immodest. The relied-upon Hanafi position (muftā bihi) is that a free adult woman's marriage without a guardian is valid and not suspended, and there is no distinction between kufu and non-kufu, though the guardian may object in non-kufu.

Key Terms
عاقلة مميزة (ʿāqila mumayyiza): A sane woman who has reached the age of discretion (tamyīz) and puberty (bulūgh).
كفو (kufu): Equality in social status, lineage, wealth, and profession between husband and wife.
ظاهر الرواية (Zahir al-Riwayah): The six core books of Imam Muhammad (al-Mabsut, al-Jami' al-Saghir, etc.) which contain the most authoritative Hanafi rulings.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position (muftā bihi), what is the ruling on a free, adult, sane woman marrying without a guardian?
A) The marriage is invalid without a guardian.
B) The marriage is valid but suspended on the guardian's permission.
C) The marriage is valid and not suspended, but the guardian may object if the husband is non-compatible.
D) The marriage is valid only if the husband is compatible.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a free adult woman can contract her own marriage; the guardian's role is social, not a legal requirement, and the marriage is valid even without his presence.

Nikah — Marriage
intermediate
8 min
Guardian Cannot Force a Virgin Adult Woman into Marriage
Inter-madhhab dispute on ijbār of adult virgin

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school prohibits forcing an adult virgin into marriage and distinguish its evidence from the Shafi'i position.

Core Idea
An adult virgin cannot be forced into marriage because she has full legal capacity; compulsion is only for minors with deficient intellect.

This passage tackles a critical intersection of gender, consent, and authority in Islamic marriage law. The Hanafi school takes a firm stand: an adult virgin woman possesses full legal capacity and cannot be coerced by her guardian. Imam al-Shafi'i disagrees, drawing an analogy to minor virgins. Understanding why the Hanafis reject this analogy reveals the centrality of intellectual maturity (taklīf) in determining legal capacity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi ruling and its basis
First, locate the core ruling: the guardian cannot force an adult virgin. Then note the primary argument: she is free (ḥurra) and has complete intellect, evidenced by being addressed by divine obligations.
The ruling is stated negatively: 'lā yajūz'—it is not permissible.
2
Understand the Shafi'i counterposition
Imam al-Shafi'i allows compulsion. His evidence is qiyas on the minor virgin: both lack experience in marriage, and the father may take her dower without explicit permission.
The common factor (ʿilla) proposed is ignorance of marriage benefits and harms.
3
Trace the Hanafi refutation
The Hanafi response distinguishes between minor and adult: the minor's deficiency is intellectual (naqṣ al-ʿaql), which ends with puberty. The adult's intellect is complete, so the analogy fails.
Refutation focuses on the ʿilla: qiyas is invalid because the cause is not present in the adult.
4
Test the objection and rebuttal
Consider the potential objection: if freedom alone prevents compulsion, then a minor is also free—why can she be forced? The answer: compulsion over the minor is due to deficient intellect, not lack of freedom.
This shows the Hanafī ijtihādī method: they avoid absolute analogies and respect context-specific causes.
5
Apply the practical outcome
The thamara (result) is clear: in Hanafi fiqh, a marriage contracted without the adult virgin's consent is suspended (mawqūf) on her approval. In Shafi'i fiqh, it is valid immediately.
This difference has direct implications for contemporary marriage contracts.

امرها ولنا انها حرة فلا يكون للغير عليها ولاية الاجبار والولاية على الصغيرة لقصور عقلها وقد كمل بالبلوغ بدليل توجه الخطاب فصار كالغلام وكالتصرف في المال وانما يملك الاب قبض الصداق برضاها دلالة ولهذا میں تصرف۔ اور مالک ہوگا مہر پر قبضہ کا اس کی رضا مندی سے۔ اور اسی وجہ سے مالک نہیں ہو گا اس کے منع کر دینے کے ساتھ ۔ تشریح مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ ہمارے نزدیک با کرہ بالغہ کو نکاح پر مجبور نہیں کیا جاسکتا ہے، چنانچہ اگر بغیر اس کی رضا مندی کے نکاح کر دیا تو اس کی اجازت پر موقوف رہے گا، اگر رد کر دیا تو باطل ہوگا اور اگر ولی کے اجازت لینے کے وقت خاموش رہی تو یہ اس کی طرف سے اجازت ہوگی۔ امام شافعی کے نزدیک با کرہ بالغہ کو نکاح پر مجبور کرنا جائز ہے۔ امام شافعی کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ وہ باکرہ بالغہ کو قیاس کرتے ہیں صغیرہ پر، اس لئے کہ صغیرہ اگر باکرہ ہے تو جس طرح اس کو نکاح پر مجبور کرنا جائز ہے اسی طرح با کرہ بالغہ کو بھی مجبور کرنا جائز ہے۔ دونوں کے درمیان جامع اور علت مشترکہ تجربہ نہ ہونے کی وجہ سے نکاح کے معاملات سے ناواقف ہونا ہے، کیونکہ عورت نے جب تک مردوں کے ساتھ ممارست نہیں کی تو وہ مصالح نکاح اور مفاسد نکاح سے واقف نہیں ہو سکتی چونکہ باکرہ بالغہ نکاح کے معاملات سے ناواقف ہے اسی وجہ سے اس کا باپ بغیر اس کے حکم کے مہر پر قبضہ کر سکتا ہے۔ ہماری دلیل یہ ہے کہ با کرہ بالغہ آزاد پر کسی کو ولایت اجبار نہیں ہوتی ہے لہذا با کرہ بالغہ پر بھی کسی کو ولایت اجبار نہیں ہوگی ۔ لیکن اگر کوئی سوال کرے کہ صغیرہ بھی تو آزاد ہے لہذا اس پر بھی ولایت اجبار نہ ہونی چاہئے ، تو اس کا جواب یہ ہے کہ صغیرہ پر ولایت اجبار اس کے قصور عقل کی وجہ سے ہے اور بالغ ہونے سے عقل مکمل ہو گئی لہذا بالغہ پر ولایت اجبار نہیں ہوگی صغیرہ پر ہوگی ۔ اور اس کی دلیل کہ بلوغ سے عقل کامل ہو جاتی ہے اللہ تعالیٰ کے خطاب کا بالغہ کی طرف متوجہ ہونا ہے چنانچہ جب تک نابالغہ ہے تو احکام شرع کی مکلف نہیں ہوگی اور
The guardian cannot force the virgin adult woman into marriage. It is not permissible for the guardian to compel the adult virgin to marry, contrary to al-Shafi'i. He analogizes to the minor, and because she is ignorant of the matter of marriage due to lack of experience, and therefore the father may take her dower without her command. And our proof is that she is free, so no one has authority to compel her; the authority over the minor was due to her defective intellect, and intellect is completed with puberty, evidenced by the address of obligations (taklif) directed to her, so she becomes like a boy and like disposal in wealth. And the father only owns the dower with her consent implicitly, and therefore he does not own it if she forbids. Translation: It is not permissible for the guardian to force the adult virgin to marry, contrary to Imam Shafi'i. Imam Shafi'i's proof is qiyas upon the minor, and this is because she is unaware of marriage matters due to lack of experience, and therefore the father may take her dower without her command. And our proof is that she is a free woman, so no one has authority of force over her; authority over the minor was due to her defective intellect. With puberty, intellect becomes complete, evidenced by the address of obligations directed to her, so forcing an adult virgin becomes like forcing an adult boy and like disposal in wealth. And he may take the dower with her consent implicitly, and therefore he cannot take it if she forbids. Explanation: According to us, an adult virgin cannot be forced into marriage; if the guardian marries her without her consent, it is suspended on her permission; if she rejects, it becomes invalid; if she remains silent when asked for permission, that counts as her permission. According to Imam Shafi'i, it is permissible to force an adult virgin. His proof is qiyas on the minor: just as a minor virgin can be forced, so too an adult virgin, because both lack experience and are unaware of the benefits harms of marriage. This is evidenced by the father taking the dower without her command. Our proof is that no one has authority of compulsion over a free person; therefore, no one has such authority over an adult virgin. If one objects: The minor is also free, so she should not be subject to compulsion either. The answer: Authority over the minor was due to her defective intellect, and with puberty intellect is perfected, so compulsion is removed for the adult. The evidence that intellect becomes complete with puberty is that the address of obligations is directed to the adult; as long as she is a minor, she is not obligated by the laws of Sharia.
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage presents the Hanafi ruling on whether a guardian may compel (ijbār) an adult virgin (bikr bāligha) into marriage. The Hanafi position is a clear prohibition: the guardian cannot force her. If he does, the marriage is suspended on her consent. Imam Shafi'i disagrees and allows compulsion. The dispute centers on qiyas (analogy). Shafi'i analogizes the adult virgin to a minor virgin, citing a common deficiency: lack of experience in marital matters. He supports this by noting that the father may take her dower without explicit permission, implying limited capacity. The Hanafi response distinguishes between the minor and the adult: the minor's intellect is deficient (qusūr al-ʿaql), so the guardian has authority to protect her interest. With adulthood, the woman attains full intellectual capacity, evidenced by being addressed by divine obligations (taklīf). Thus, she is like a free adult male—no one has authority to compel him in marriage. The father's right to take the dower implicitly is based on presumed consent, but if she explicitly forbids, he cannot take it. The text also addresses an objection: if a minor is also free, why can she be forced? The answer: compulsion for the minor is due to deficient intellect, not lack of freedom; once intellect is complete, compulsion ends. This ruling is Zahir al-Riwayah and muftā bihi in the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
بكر بالغة (bikr bāligha): A virgin woman who has reached puberty (bulūgh) and is adult.
اجبار (ijbār): Compulsion or forcing someone into a contract against their will.
ولاية الاجبار (wilāyat al-ijbār): Authority of the guardian to compel the ward into marriage without consent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a guardian marries an adult virgin without her consent, what is the status of the marriage?
A) The marriage is valid immediately.
B) The marriage is invalid and cannot be ratified.
C) The marriage is suspended on her consent; if she rejects, it becomes invalid.
D) The marriage is valid but the guardian commits a sin.

Key Takeaway
An adult virgin's full legal capacity precludes forced marriage; compulsion is only justified for those with deficient intellect, such as minors or the insane.

Guardian Cannot Force Mature Virgin into Marriage
Ruling prohibiting compulsion with inter-madhhab dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on guardian's compulsion of a mature virgin and distinguish it from the Shafi'i position, citing the Hanafi proof.

Core Idea
A mature virgin cannot be forced into marriage—her maturity nullifies the guardian’s right of compulsion.

In this lesson, we examine a key dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools: whether a guardian can compel a mature virgin (bikr balighah) to marry. The passage from Al-Hidayah presents the Hanafi position that compulsion is not allowed, and refutes the Shafi'i analogy with minors. By the end, you will understand the legal reasoning based on maturity and freedom.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Plain Ruling
Read the first line: 'و لا يجوز للولى اجبار البكر البالغة على النكاح' – the ruling is clear: compulsion is not permissible.
This is the mufta bihi position.
2
State the Shafi'i Objection
The next phrase 'خلافا للشافعى' introduces the opposing view. Al-Shafi'i analogizes the mature virgin to a minor, claiming both lack experience.
3
Analyze the Hanafi Proof
The Hanafi evidence begins with 'ولنا انها حرة' – she is free and mature. Maturity brings full legal capacity (taklif), so no one can compel her, just like a mature male or property disposal.
The proof is rational (istidlal aqli).
4
Understand the Refutation of the Analogy
The Shafi'i analogy fails because the minor's compulsion was due to intellectual deficiency, which is removed by maturity. The father's taking of dower is only with implied consent, not compulsion.
If she explicitly forbids, the father cannot take the dower – 'لا يملك مع نهيها'.
5
Apply the Practical Difference
The dispute has real consequences: a marriage contracted without consent is suspended (mawquf) under Hanafi law, but valid under Shafi'i. Know which school you are following.
This is thamarah al-khilaf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و لا يجوز للولى اجبار البكر البالغة على النكاح خلافا للشافعى له الاعتبار بالصغيرة ولهذا لانها جاهلة بامر النكاح لعدم التجربة ولهذا يقبض الاب صداقها بغير امرها ولنا انها حرة فلا يكون للغير عليها ولاية الاجبار والولاية على الصغيرة لقصور عقلها وقد كمل بالبلوغ بدليل توجه الخطاب فصار كالغلام وكالتصرف في المال وانما يملك الاب قبض الصداق برضائها دلالة ولهذا لايـملك مع نهيها
And it is not permissible for the guardian to compel the mature virgin to marry, contrary to al-Shafi'i. His (al-Shafi'i) analogy is with the minor, and because she is ignorant of the matter of marriage due to lack of experience, and for this reason the father takes possession of her dower without her command. And our proof is that she is free, so no one has authority of compulsion over her. And authority over the minor is due to deficiency of her intellect, and she has become complete with maturity, evidenced by the address (of divine law) being directed to her. So she becomes like a mature male, and like disposition of property. And the father only possesses the right to take the dower with her implied consent, and for this reason he does not possess it when she explicitly forbids.
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof addressing the issue of whether a guardian (wali) can compel a mature virgin (bikr balighah) into marriage. The Hanafi position is that compulsion (ijbar) is not allowed, contrary to Imam al-Shafi'i. The passage first presents the Shafi'i argument: he compares the mature virgin to a minor virgin, arguing that both lack experience in marital matters, and thus the guardian's authority extends to compulsion. As evidence, the Shafi'is point out that the father takes the dower on her behalf without her explicit command, showing his authority. Then the Hanafi response is given: the mature virgin is free and has attained maturity, which brings full legal capacity, as evidenced by the fact that divine obligations (taklif) are addressed to her. The authority of compulsion over minors existed only due to their intellectual deficiency, which is removed by maturity. Therefore, the mature virgin is like a mature male—no one can force him to marry—and like her own property: she has full disposition over her wealth. As for the father taking the dower, that is only based on her implied consent (dalalatan); if she explicitly forbids, he cannot take it. This ruling is from the Zahir al-Riwayah and is the relied-upon position.

Key Terms
بکر بالغہ (bikr balighah): A virgin woman who has attained puberty and is of sound mind; in this context, one who is legally mature.
ولایت اجبار (wilayat al-ijbar): The authority of a guardian to force a ward into marriage without their consent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because she is a minor.
B) Because she is free and mature, and no one has authority of compulsion over a free mature person.
C) Because the guardian's permission is required.
D) Because she must give explicit verbal consent.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that a mature virgin's full legal capacity overrides any guardian's right of compulsion, contrary to the Shafi'i analogy with minors.

Silence or Laughter of Mature Virgin Indicates Consent
Ruling on virgin's consent via silence or laughter

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a mature virgin's silence or genuine laughter constitutes valid consent in marriage, and distinguish it from responses that do not indicate consent.

Core Idea
A virgin's silence or genuine laughter is valid consent; crying or mocking laughter is not.

This lesson explores the Hanafi position on obtaining a virgin’s consent in marriage. The Prophet ﷺ taught that a virgin is consulted and her silence indicates consent. The text from Al-Hidayah explains that silence, due to modesty, is a valid indication, and laughter is even stronger evidence. However, exceptions like mocking laughter or crying are distinguished.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the act being ruled upon? The guardian seeking permission from a mature virgin, and her response (silence or laughter) as consent. The ruling is that such responses constitute valid permission.
2
Locate the dalil
What proof is cited? The hadith: 'البكر تستأمر في نفسها فان سكتت فقد رضيت' (The virgin is consulted about herself; if she remains silent, she has consented).
This is an ahad hadith, so it is zanni al-thubut, but still binding.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
How does silence indicate consent? The reason is that the virgin is shy (haya') to express desire, but not shy to reject. Thus, silence is interpreted as consent. Laughter is even stronger evidence because it shows joy.
The predominance of consent (rajah) over rejection is key.
4
Note the exceptions and nuances
Crying openly is evidence of displeasure and thus rejection. Mocking laughter (mustahzi'ah) does not count as consent. Crying without sound is not considered rejection.
Intra-Hanafi difference: some scholars hold that mocking laughter invalidates consent.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position is that silence and genuine laughter are valid consent. Crying openly is rejection. Mocking laughter is not consent.
This is the position in Zahir al-Riwayah as presented in Al-Hidayah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال فاذا استأذنها الولى فسكتت او ضحكت فهو اذن لقوله عليه السلام البكر تستأمر في نفسها فان سكتت فقد رضيت ولأن جهة الرضاء فيه راجحة لانها تستحيى عن اظهار الرغبة لا عن الرد والضحك ادل على الرضاء من السكوت بخلاف ما اذا بكت لانه دليل السخط والكراهة وقيل اذا ضحكت كالمستهزية بما سمعت لايكون رضا واذا بكت بلا صوت لم يكن ردا
He said: When the guardian seeks permission from her, and she remains silent or laughs, then it is permission, due to his statement (peace be upon him): 'The virgin is consulted about herself; if she remains silent, then she has consented.' And because the aspect of consent is predominant in it, for she is shy to show desire, not to reject. And laughing is more indicative of consent than silence. Contrary to when she cries, because it is evidence of displeasure and dislike. And it has been said: if she laughs as if mocking what she heard, it is not consent. And if she cries without sound, it is not a rejection.
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof clarifying how a guardian should seek consent from a mature virgin and what constitutes valid consent. The ruling is based on a hadith of the Prophet ﷺ that the virgin is consulted about herself; if she remains silent, that indicates consent. The reason is that a virgin, due to modesty (haya'), may be reluctant to express desire verbally but would not be shy to reject if she disapproves. Therefore, silence is interpreted as consent. Laughter is even stronger evidence because it shows joy and approval. However, if she cries, that indicates dislike and is not consent. An exception is noted: if she laughs mockingly (mustahzi'ah), that does not count as consent. Also, crying without sound (no audible weeping) is not considered rejection; it may be due to modesty. This is a detailed ruling from the Hanafi school, based on the Prophetic hadith and rational consideration of predominant inclination. The mufta bihi position is that silence or laughter (not mocking) is consent, while crying openly is a clear rejection.

Key Terms
سکوت (sukut): The virgin's lack of verbal response when asked about marriage, which indicates consent in Hanafi law.
ضحک (dahk): Audible laughter as a response, considered stronger indication of consent than silence.
بكى (baka): Crying; in the context of consent, crying with sound indicates rejection, while crying without sound is not considered a rejection.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is a clear rejection.
B) It is considered consent.
C) It is invalid and the guardian must ask again.
D) It nullifies the marriage.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a virgin's silence or genuine laughter upon being asked for consent is considered valid consent, while crying or mocking laughter is not.

The Mature Virgin’s Autonomy in Marriage and Rebuttal of Shafi’i
Stating independence of mature virgin and rebutting Shafi’i on mahr seizure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on a mature virgin’s capacity to marry and respond to the Shafi’i counter-argument using the principle of silence as indication of consent.

Core Idea
A mature virgin has full authority; her silence implies consent, but explicit objection overrides it.

The Hanafi school grants a mature virgin (bākira bāligha) complete legal autonomy in both marriage and financial matters. This lesson examines that ruling and addresses a potential objection from Imam Shafi’i, who argued that the father may take the mahr without her explicit command. Through this dispute, we clarify the role of silence as an indicator of consent and the limits of guardianship.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi position
State the ruling on a mature virgin's capacity in marriage and property: she is like a mature boy, no compulsory guardianship, she has full authority over herself and her property.
This is the starting point for the dispute.
2
Identify the Shafi’i objection
Imam Shafi’i (as presented) holds that the father may take possession of the mahr without the daughter's explicit command.
The opposing school's claim is based on an alternative view of guardianship.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response uses the principle that silence indicates consent (dalālat al-riḍā). The father's act is valid only due to this implied consent; if she explicitly forbids, his right ceases.
This shows that consent is essential, even if implied.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: Under Hanafi, a daughter can prevent her father from taking the mahr by explicitly objecting; under Shafi’i, she cannot.
This highlights the different weight given to explicit vs. implied consent.
5
Confirm the mufta bihi
The Hanafi position is the relied-upon (mufta bihi) view, based on the mature woman's full legal capacity.
This ruling is from the zahir al-riwayah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
جب بالغ ہو گئی تو مکلف ہو جائے گی۔ اور بالغہ لڑکی ایسی ہے جیسا کہ بالغ لڑکا، یعنی جس طرح بالغ لڑکے پر ولایت اجبہ نہیں اسی طرح بالغہ لڑکی پر بھی ولایت اجبار نہیں اور یہ ایسا ہے جیسا کہ تصرف فی المال یعنی جس طرح باکرہ بالغہ اپنے مال میں تصرف کر سکتی ہے اس کے باپ کیلئے اس کے مال میں تصرف کی اجازت نہیں، اسی طرح با کرہ بالغہ اپنے نفس میں بھی تصرف کی مختار ہوگی ، باپ یا دوسرے ولی کی اجازت شرط نہیں۔
رہا امام شافعی کا یہ کہنا کہ باپ بغیر اس کے حکم کے مہر پر قبضہ کر لیا ہے تو ہم جوابا یہ کہتے ہیں کہ جب باپ نے مہر پر قبضہ کیا تو لڑکی کے سکوت کی وجہ سے دلالہ رضامندی پائی گئی ، چنانچہ اگرلڑکی نے صراحة منع کر دیا تو باپ مہر پر قبضہ کا مالک نہیں ہوگا۔
When she becomes mature, she becomes responsible. And a mature girl is like a mature boy, meaning just as there is no compulsory guardianship over a mature boy, likewise there is no compulsory guardianship over a mature girl. And this is like disposal of property: just as a mature virgin can dispose of her property and her father has no permission to dispose of her property, similarly a mature virgin has authority over her own person; the permission of father or other guardian is not required. As for Imam Shafi’i’s statement that the father has taken possession of the mahr without her order, we reply: when the father took possession of the mahr, due to the girl’s silence, indication of consent is found. Therefore, if the girl explicitly forbids, the father will not be owner of the mahr.
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage establishes the Hanafi ruling that a mature virgin (baakirah baalighah) has full legal capacity in her own marriage, just as in financial matters. No guardian, not even the father, can force her into a marriage (wilayat ijbar ends with maturity). The passage then addresses a possible objection from Imam Shafi’i, who apparently argued that the father may take the mahr without the daughter’s explicit command. The Hanafi response clarifies that if the father takes the mahr, the daughter’s silence indicates implied consent, but if she explicitly objects, the father has no right. This confirms that the mature virgin’s independent consent is paramount, and the father’s authority over her property is also limited by her explicit will.

Key Terms
ولایت اجبار (wilayat ijbar): Compulsory guardianship over a minor or insane person to marry them without their consent.
باکرہ بالغہ (baakirah baalighah): A virgin who has reached puberty (maturity).
سکوت (sakut): Silence from the woman when asked for permission, which is considered an indication of consent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway

Consent by Silence or Laughter of a Mature Virgin
Ruling on consent indicators for a mature virgin

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a mature virgin's silence or genuine laughter upon consultation constitutes valid consent and explain the evidence from hadith and legal reasoning.

Core Idea
A mature virgin's silence or genuine laughter is valid consent because shyness impedes verbal acceptance.

This lesson examines a key ruling in Hanafi marriage law: how a mature virgin (bikr balighah) can indicate her consent to marriage. The passage from Al-Hidayah establishes that when her guardian consults her, silence or genuine laughter counts as permission (idhn). We will explore the hadith proof, the rational basis rooted in female modesty, and the exceptions of mocking laughter and crying.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the guardian seeking permission (isti'mar) from a mature virgin. The ruling: if she is silent or laughs (non-mocking), it is valid consent (sahih).
The hukm level is sahih, meaning the marriage contract is valid with such consent.
2
Locate the dalil (proof)
The author cites the hadith: 'البكر تستامر في نفسها فان سكتت فقد رضيت' — 'The virgin is consulted regarding herself; if she is silent, then she has consented.'
This is a sunnah ahad, zanni al-thubut, but relied upon in Hanafi fiqh.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah (legal implication)
The word 'سكتت' (she is silent) is directly linked to 'رضيت' (she consented). The imperative 'تستامر' indicates obligation to consult. Laughter is included by qiyas because it shows stronger consent.
The rational proof: a virgin is shy of expressing desire but not refusal; hence absence of refusal indicates consent.
4
Note the exceptions and nuances
Mocking laughter is not consent. Crying without sound is not refusal, because tears can be involuntary.
These nuances ensure the ruling is applied correctly in practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال فاذا استاذ نها الولى فسكتت او ضحكت فهو اذن لقوله عليه السلام البكر تستامر في نفسها فان سكتت فقد رضيت ولأن جهة الرضاء فيه راجحة لانها تستحيى عن اظهار الرغبة لا عن الرد والضحك ادل على الرضاء من السكوت بخلاف ما اذا بكت لانه دليل السخط والكراهة وقيل اذا ضحكت كالمستهزية بما سمعت لايكون رضا واذا بكت بلا صوت لم يكن ردا
ترجمہ ۔ پس جب ولی نے باکرہ بالغہ سے اجازت طلب کی پھر وہ چپ ہوگئی یا ہنس پڑی تو یہ اجازت ہے ۔ حضور صلى الله عليه وسلم کے قول لیکر الحدیث کی وجہ سے یعنی باکرہ سے اس کی ذات کے بارے میں اجازت لیجائے اگر خاموش ہو گئی تو وہ راضی ہے کیونکہ خاموش رہنے میں رضامندی کی جہت غالب ہے اس لئے کہ وہ صاف رغبت کرنے سے شرم کرتی ہے نہ کہ انکار کرنے سے ۔ اور ہنسنا خاموش رہنے سے بڑھ کر رضا مندی کی دلیل ہے بخلاف اس کے جب وہ رونے لگی اس لئے کہ یہ نا خوشی اور ناگواری کی دلیل ہے اور کہا گیا کہ جب ہنسی اس طرح کہ سنی ہوئی بات کا استہزاء کرنے والی ہے تو یہ رضا مندی شمار نہیں ہوگا اور جب بغیر آواز کے رونے لگی تو یہ رونا رد اور انکار نہیں ہوگا۔
تشریح مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ نکاح سے پہلے ولی نے باکرہ بالغہ سے اجازت مانگی وہ خاموش ہوگئی یا ہنس پڑی تو یہ خاموش رہنا یا ہنسنا اس کی طرف سے اجازت ہوگا اسی طرح تبسم بھی رضا کی دلیل ہے۔ صاحب ہدایہ نے دلیل میں حضور کا ارشاد نقل فرمایا کہ با کرہ سے اس کی ذات کے بارے میں رائے لی جائے پس اگر وہ چپ رہی تو یہ رضامندی ہے۔ دلیل عقلی یہ ہے کہ چپ رہنے اور ہنسنے میں رضا مندی کی جہت غالب ہے۔ کیونکہ باکرہ اظہار رغبت سے تو شرماتی ہے انکار کرنے سے نہیں لہذا اگر وہ ناراض ہوتی تو صاف صاف انکار کر دیتی لیکن انکار نہ کرنا دلیل ہے اس کی رضا مندی کی ۔ اگر آپ کو یہ اشکال پیدا ہو کہ حدیث میں سکوت کا بیان تو ہے لیکن ضحک کا نہیں پھر آپ نے ضحک کو دلیل رضا کیونکر بنایا۔ صاحب ہدایہ نے جواب دیا کہ ضحک سکوت کے مقابلہ میں رضامندی پر زیادہ دلالت کرتا ہے۔ اس لئے کہ ضحک قول مسموع پر سرور اور فرحت کی علامت ہے، پس جب سکوت دلیل رضا ہے تو ضحک دلیل رضا بدرجہ اولی ہوگا، البتہ با کرہ کا رونا رضامندی کی دلیل نہیں بلکہ نا خوشی اور ناگواری کی دلیل ہے۔ جن حضرات کی رائے یہ ہے کہ اگر با کرہ استہزاء کے انداز پر ہنسے تو یہ ہنسنا با کرہ کی رضا مندی نہیں کہلائے گا۔ کیونکہ یہ ولی کی بات کا تمسخر ہوگا نہ کہ اجازت۔ اور اگر با کرہ بغیر آواز کے رونے لگے تو یہ اس کی طرف سے انکار نہیں کہلائے گا۔ کیوں کہ بسا اوقات ماں باپ کی جدائی کی خبر پر غیر اختیاری آنسو نکل آتے ہیں۔ اس پر فتویٰ ہے بلکہ دیکھنے میں آیا ہے کہ کبھی کبھی خوشی کی خبر پر بھی آنسو نکل آتے ہیں، لہذا اس بلا آواز کے رونے کو انکار اور رد نہیں سمجھا جائے گا۔
He said: When the guardian seeks permission from her, and she is silent or laughs, then it is permission, because of his saying, peace be upon him: 'The virgin is consulted regarding herself; if she is silent, then she has consented.' And because the aspect of consent is predominant therein, because she is shy of showing desire, not of refusal. And laughter is more indicative of consent than silence. Contrary to when she cries, because that is evidence of displeasure and dislike. And it is said: When she laughs scornfully at what she heard, it is not consent. And when she cries without sound, it is not refusal.
Translation: When the guardian sought permission from the mature virgin, and she became silent or laughed, then this is permission. Because of the hadith of the Prophet (peace be upon him): 'The virgin is consulted regarding herself; if she is silent, then she has consented.' Because in silence the aspect of consent is predominant, for she is shy of showing desire explicitly, not of refusal. And laughter is more indicative of consent than silence. Contrary to when she cries, because that indicates displeasure. And it is said: if she laughs in a mocking manner at what she heard, that is not considered consent. And if she cries without sound, it is not refusal.
Commentary: The issue is that before marriage, when the guardian asks permission from a mature virgin, she becomes silent or laughs, then this silence or laughter is considered permission from her. Similarly, a smile is also evidence of consent. In the proof, the author of Hidayah quotes the Prophet's statement: 'Consult the virgin about herself; if she is silent, that is consent.' The rational proof is that in silence and laughter, the aspect of consent is predominant, because a virgin is shy of expressing desire but not of refusal. If she were displeased, she would explicitly refuse; not refusing indicates consent. If one objects that the hadith mentions silence only, not laughter, then how is laughter made evidence of consent? The author of Hidayah replies that laughter is even more indicative of consent than silence, because laughter is a sign of joy and happiness upon hearing the proposal. Since silence is evidence, laughter is even more so. However, a virgin's crying is not evidence of consent; rather it indicates displeasure. Some scholars say that if a virgin laughs in a mocking manner, that laughter is not considered her consent, because it is mockery of the guardian's statement, not consent. And if a virgin cries without sound, it is not considered refusal, because sometimes tears come involuntarily upon hearing of separation from parents; indeed, sometimes tears come even upon hearing happy news. Therefore, crying without sound is not understood as refusal or rejection. This is the fatwa position.
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling from the matn of Al-Hidayah regarding how a mature virgin’s consent is obtained in marriage. The guardian asks her permission; if she remains silent or laughs, that counts as permission (idhn). The proof is a hadith: 'The virgin is consulted regarding herself; if she is silent, that is consent.' The rational basis is that a virgin is naturally shy of expressing desire but not of refusal; therefore, the absence of refusal indicates consent. Laughter is an even stronger indicator because it shows joy, whereas silence might be ambiguous. However, not all laughter counts—if she laughs mockingly, it is not consent. Crying, whether with or without sound, is generally not consent; crying without sound is not considered refusal because emotional tears can occur for various reasons. The commentary also addresses a potential objection that the hadith only mentions silence, not laughter. The response is that laughter is more indicative of consent by primary analogy (biddaraja al-ula).

Key Terms
الاستيمار (al-isti'mar): The act of seeking permission or consultation from a woman regarding her marriage.
سکوت (sukut): Silence, which is considered a valid indication of consent when the guardian asks the virgin.
ضحک (dahk): Laughter, which is even stronger evidence of consent than silence, unless it is mocking.
بکاء (buka): Crying. Crying with sound indicates displeasure (not consent); crying without sound is not considered refusal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is laughter considered a stronger indication of consent than silence?
A) Because the hadith explicitly mentions laughter.
B) Because laughter shows joy and happiness, which is a clearer sign of acceptance.
C) Because silence could mean indifference.
D) Because laughter is louder.

Key Takeaway
Silence or genuine laughter of a mature virgin upon guardian's consultation is valid consent in Hanafi fiqh, supported by hadith and the principle of shyness.

Consent When Asked by Someone Other Than the Guardian
Explaining that a mature virgin's silence or laughter does not constitute consent when asked by a non-guardian or a distant guardian.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a mature virgin's silence or laughter when asked by a non-guardian or distant guardian, and explain the rationale based on necessity (hajah).

Core Idea
Silence is consent only from the guardian; with strangers, she must speak.

The Hanafi school has a well-known rule: a mature virgin's silence or laughter in response to a marriage proposal is considered her consent. However, this rule has an important condition: it only applies when the asker is her proper guardian (wali) or his authorized messenger. In this lesson, we explore why this condition exists and how it affects the validity of consent when someone else—a non-guardian or a distant guardian—makes the request.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule and its exception
Recall that a mature virgin's silence (sukut) or laughter (dahk) normally indicates consent. But this lesson shows that this only works when asked by the proper guardian. Determine the two categories of people for whom silence is NOT valid consent.
Non-guardian (ghayr wali) and distant guardian (wali ab'ad) when a closer guardian exists.
2
Understand the rationale: necessity (hajah)
Why does the school accept silence from the guardian? Because the virgin feels shy (haya) to speak in front of him, so there is a need to rely on non-verbal indication. With a stranger, she has no such shyness, so no necessity exists.
The concept of hajah is key: reliance on ambiguous evidence is allowed only when necessary.
3
Apply the exception: the guardian's messenger
The messenger (rasul) stands in the guardian's place. Since the messenger represents the guardian, the same necessity applies, so silence is acceptable.
The messenger is considered equivalent to the guardian for the purpose of consent.
4
Distinguish between valid and invalid consent scenarios
Create two cases: (1) Guardian or his messenger asks → silence is consent. (2) Non-guardian or distant guardian asks → she must verbally consent. Identify the practical consequence: if she remains silent in case (2), the marriage contract would be invalid because consent was not established.
Verbal consent is always valid, but silence is only valid in the specific case.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وان فعل هذا غير الولی یعنی استامر غير الولی او ولی غیره اولى منه لم يكن رضا حتى تتكلم به لان هذا السكوت لقلة الالتفات الى كلامه فلم يقع دلالة على الرضاء ولو وقع فهو محتمل والاكتفاء بمثله للحاجة ولاحاجة في حق غير الاولياء بخلاف ما اذاكان المستامر رسول الولى لانه قائم مقامه
ترجمہ اور اگر کیا یہ غیر ولی (اجنبی ) نے یعنی اجازت طلب کی غیر ولی نے یا ایسے ولی نے کہ اسکے علاوہ اس سے زیادہ قریب ہے تو رضا مندی نہیں ہوگی۔ یہاں تک کہ رضا کا کلام کرے اسلئے کہ یہ سکوت قلت التفات کی وجہ سے ہے اس کے کلام کی طرف پس رضامندی پر دلالت واقع نہیں ہوگی اور اگر واقع بھی ہوگئی تو محتمل ہے اور اکتفاء اس جیسی پر حاجت کی وجہ سے ہے اور غیر اولیاء کے حق میں کوئی حاجت نہیں ۔ بخلاف اس صور کیے کہ استیمار لینے والا ولی کا قاصد ہے اس لئے کہ ولی کا قاصد ولی کے قائم مقام ہے۔
تشریح مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ اگر با کرہ بالغہ سے غیر ولی نے اجازت طلب کی یا ولی اقرب کی موجودگی میں ولی ابعد نے نکاح کی اجازت مانگی تو ان دونوں صورتوں میں سکوت یا ضحک رضامندی کی دلیل نہیں ہوگا بلکہ اجازت دینے کے لئے زبان سے کلام کرنا ضروری ہوگا کیونکہ اجنبی یا ولی ابعد کی بات کے جواب میں خاموشی التفات نہ کرنے کی وجہ سے ہے جس کو دلیل رضا نہیں کہا جا سکتا۔ اور اگر تھوڑی دیر کے لئے مان بھی لیا جائے کہ خاموشی بھی دلیل رضا ہے تو احتمال اس میں عدم رضا کا بھی ہے اور اس جیسی رضا مندی پر اکتفاء حاجت کی وجہ سے ہوتا ہے اور غیر اولیاء کے حق میں کوئی حاجت نہیں، کیونکہ باکرہ بالغہ غیر اولیاء کے سامنے کلام کرنے سے شرم نہیں کرے گی ہاں البتہ اگر اجازت لینے والا ولی کا قاصد ہے تو وہ ولی کے قائم مقام ہو کر ولی کے حکم میں ہوگا۔
And if this is done by other than the guardian, i.e., a non-guardian asks permission, or a guardian who is not the closest, then it is not consent until she speaks it. Because this silence is due to lack of attention to his speech, so it does not indicate consent. Even if it occurs, it is ambiguous, and relying on such [silence] is due to necessity, and there is no necessity in the case of non-guardians. Contrary to when the one asking is the messenger of the guardian, because he stands in his place.
Translation: And if this is done by other than the guardian, i.e., a non-guardian asks permission, or a guardian who is not the closest, then consent will not be established until she speaks it. This silence is due to lack of attention to his speech, so it does not indicate consent. Even if it were to indicate, it would be ambiguous, and relying on such ambiguity is due to necessity, but there is no necessity regarding non-guardians. Contrary to the case where the one seeking permission is the messenger of the guardian, because the messenger stands in the guardian's place.
Explanation: If someone other than the guardian asks permission from a mature virgin, or a distant guardian asks in the presence of a closer guardian, then her silence or laughter will not be evidence of consent. Rather, she must speak her consent. This is because silence in response to a stranger or distant guardian is due to lack of attention, not consent. Even if silence were considered evidence, it would be ambiguous, and reliance on such ambiguous evidence is only due to necessity (i.e., to avoid embarrassment with one's guardian). Since she is not shy to speak in front of non-guardians, no necessity exists. However, if the person asking is the guardian's messenger, he takes the guardian's place and thus silence is acceptable.
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage clarifies a key limitation: the ruling that silence or laughter indicates consent only applies when the person seeking permission is the proper guardian (wali) or his authorized messenger. If a stranger (ajnabi) or a distant guardian (wali ab'ad) asks, silence is not considered consent – verbal expression is required. The reasoning is that a virgin feels shy to speak in front of her guardian, prompting necessity (hajah) to accept non-verbal indications. But with strangers, she has no such shyness, so verbal consent is needed. The distant guardian also falls under this because his proximity is less than the nearer guardian; she may not feel the same shame. The exception is the guardian's messenger, who stands in the guardian's place.

Key Terms
غیر ولی (ghayr wali): Anyone who is not the legal guardian (wali) of the woman in marriage, including distant relatives who are not the closest guardian.
ولی ابعد (wali ab'ad): A guardian who is more distant in the hierarchy of guardianship, e.g., a paternal uncle when the father is present.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a mature virgin’s paternal cousin (not her guardian) asks her permission for marriage and she remains silent, what is the ruling according to Hanafi fiqh?
A) Her silence is considered consent.
B) She must verbally consent; silence is not enough.
C) She must laugh for it to be consent.
D) The cousin can marry her anyway.

Key Takeaway
A mature virgin's silence or laughter is valid consent only when the asker is her proper guardian or his messenger; otherwise, verbal consent is required.

Informed Consent: Identifying the Husband, Not the Mahr
Stating the two rules for seeking virgin's consent

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two requirements for seeking a virgin's consent in Hanafi fiqh: that the husband must be identified, and that mentioning the mahr is not required.

Core Idea
Informed consent requires knowing who the husband is, but the mahr can be settled later.

When a guardian seeks consent from a virgin (bikr) for marriage, the Hanafi school sets specific parameters to ensure her consent is informed and free. The focus is on her knowledge of the groom's identity, not on financial details. This passage from the Hidayah clarifies that while identifying the husband is essential, specifying the mahr at that stage is not a condition. Let's examine the reasoning behind these rules.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two rulings
Read the passage and pinpoint the two distinct legal statements: one about tasmiyat al-zawj (naming the husband) and one about tasmiyat al-mahr (naming the mahr).
The first ruling is positive (required), the second is negative (not required).
2
Understand why husband identification is required
The text says 'so that her desire for him becomes distinct from her disinterest.' Consent must be based on knowledge of the specific person. Without identification, you cannot know if she truly accepts that particular groom.
This is about informed choice, a key principle in nikah.
3
Recognize why mahr mention is not required
The passage gives the reason: 'because marriage is valid without it.' In Hanafi fiqh, the marriage contract is valid even if the mahr is deferred or unspecified (mahr al-mithl applies). Therefore, mentioning it at consent stage is not a condition.
This does not mean mahr is optional; it remains wajib but can be determined later.
4
Connect to broader Hanafi principles
The rule reflects the Hanafi emphasis on the validity of the contract (sihhat al-nikah) without specification of mahr, contrasting with some other schools that require mahr to be known.
This is an example of istihsan (juristic preference) over strict qiyas.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتعتبر في الاستيمار تسمية الزوج على وجه تقع به المعرفة لتظهر رغبتها فيه من رغبتها عنه ولا تشترط تسمية المهر هو الصحيح لان النكاح صحيح بدونه
ترجمہ اور اعتبار کیا جائے گا اجازت لینے میں شوہر کا ذکر ایسے طریقہ پر کہ اس سے شوہر کی شناخت ہو جائے تا کہ اس میں باکرہ بالغہ کی رغبت ، اس کی بے رغبتی سے ممتاز ہو جائے ۔ اور نہیں شرط لگائی گئی مہر کے ذکر کی، یہی صحیح ہے۔ اسلئے کہ نکاح بغیر مہر کے بھی درست ہے۔
تشریح باکرہ بالغہ سے اجازت لیتے وقت شوہر کا ایسا تعارف کرا دیا جائے کہ وہ دوسروں سے ممتاز ہو سکے اور یہ معلوم ہو جائے کہ باکرہ بالغہ اس زوج مسمی سے رغبت کرتی ہے یا بے رغبتی ، البتہ اجازت کے وقت مہر کا ذکر کرنا ضروری نہیں کیونکہ نکاح بغیر ذکر مہر کے بھی
And in seeking permission, the naming of the husband is considered in a way that identification occurs, so that her desire for him becomes distinct from her disinterest. And the naming of the mahr is not a condition; that is correct, because marriage is valid without it.
Translation: When seeking permission, the husband must be mentioned in such a way that he can be identified, so that the mature virgin’s desire or disinterest becomes clear. Mentioning the mahr is not a condition; this is the correct view, because marriage is valid without specifying the mahr.
Explanation: When seeking permission from a mature virgin, the prospective husband should be introduced in a way that distinguishes him from others, so that it can be known whether she desires this specific husband or not. However, mentioning the mahr at the time of seeking permission is not necessary, because marriage is valid without mentioning the mahr.
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage addresses the procedure of isti'mar (seeking consent). It establishes two points: First, the woman must be informed of the identity of the prospective husband so that her consent is informed and based on knowledge of the specific person. Second, it is not required to mention the mahr at the time of seeking permission, because the marriage contract itself is valid without specifying the mahr (though a mahr is ultimately obligatory). This is consistent with the Hanafi position that marriage is valid with deferred or unspecified mahr, which is then determined by proper mahr (mahr al-mithl).

Key Terms
تسمية الزوج (tasmiyat al-zawj): Mentioning the name and identification of the prospective husband when seeking the woman's consent.
تسمية المهر (tasmiyat al-mahr): Mentioning the amount or nature of the mahr at the time of seeking consent or contracting marriage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What must be disclosed to a mature virgin when seeking her consent for marriage according to Hanafi fiqh?
A) The exact amount of mahr.
B) The identity of the prospective husband.
C) Both the mahr and the husband's identity.
D) Only the guardian's approval.

Key Takeaway
When seeking a virgin's consent, ensure she knows the groom's identity; the mahr can be determined later without affecting the validity of her consent.

Consent from Non-Wali: Silence Insufficient; Mahr Not Required
Rulings on seeking consent from a virgin by a non-wali

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state when a virgin's silence constitutes valid consent and explain why mentioning mahr is not required when seeking consent.

Core Idea
Silence is only consent when asked by a wali or his messenger; mahr need not be mentioned.

This passage from Al-Hidayah clarifies two important points in the marriage consent procedure: the role of the questioner and the mention of mahr. Understanding these distinctions prevents invalid marriages based on ambiguous consent.

Guided Path
1
Identify the questioner
Determine who is asking for consent: a non-wali (or distant wali) or the wali's messenger. The ruling on silence differs.
Non-wali includes any relative who is not the closest guardian or a stranger.
2
Apply the ruling on silence
If the questioner is other than the wali, silence is not consent—she must speak. If the questioner is the wali's messenger, silence is consent.
The rationale is necessity: shyness before a wali is expected, so silence implies consent; before a non-wali, her silence likely indicates disregard.
3
Check husband identification
The husband must be identified sufficiently during the consent process so her preference is clear.
Vague references like 'a man' are insufficient.
4
Note mahr exclusion
Mentioning the mahr is not a condition for valid consent. The marriage contract is valid without specifying mahr.
Some later scholars recommended mentioning mahr as it may affect preference, but it is not required.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال وان فعل هذا غير الولی یعنی استامر غير الولی او ولی غیره اولى منه لم يكن رضا حتى تتكلم به لان هذا السكوت لقلة الالتفات الى كلامه فلم يقع دلالة على الرضاء ولو وقع فهو محتمل والاكتفاء بمثله للحاجة ولاحاجة في حق غير الاولياء بخلاف ما اذاكان المستامر رسول الولى لانه قائم مقامه وتعتبر في الاستيمار تسمية الزوج على وجه تقع به المعرفة لتظهر رغبتها فيه من رغبتها عنه ولا تشترط تسمية المهر هو الصحيح لان النكاح صحيح بدونه
He said: And if this is done by other than the wali, i.e., a non-wali seeks permission, or a wali who is more distant than another wali, it will not be consent until she speaks, because this silence is due to lack of attention to his speech, so it does not indicate consent. And even if it did indicate, it is probable, and contenting with such is due to necessity, and there is no necessity regarding non-walis. Unlike if the one seeking permission is the messenger of the wali, for he stands in his place. And it is considered in seeking permission to mention the husband in a manner that gives recognition, so that her desire for him or her aversion becomes apparent. And it is not a condition to mention the dower; this is the correct view, because marriage is valid without it.
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage from Al-Hidayah states two rulings regarding seeking consent from a virgin (bikr) for marriage. First, if someone other than her guardian (wali) asks for permission—whether a complete stranger or a relative who is not her closest guardian—her silence does not constitute consent. She must explicitly verbalize her agreement. The reason is that her silence likely stems from ignoring the speaker rather than indicating approval. Even if silence could imply consent, it is ambiguous, and accepting ambiguous consent is only justified by necessity (e.g., modesty before a wali). Since she has no such inhibition before a non-wali, necessity does not apply. However, if the questioner is the wali's messenger, he is treated like the wali, and silence is consent. Second, when asking for consent, the prospective husband must be identified well enough that the woman can distinguish him. Specifying the mahr is not required, because marriage contracts are valid without mentioning mahr. Some later scholars recommended mentioning mahr as it affects preference, but the relied-upon view is that it is not a condition.

Key Terms
الولي (al-wali): The legal guardian who has authority to contract marriage for a woman.
السكوت (al-sukut): Silence; in this context, the response (or lack thereof) when asked for consent to marriage.
المهر (al-mahr): The dower given by the husband to the wife as a mandatory gift.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, if a stranger (non-wali) asks a virgin for her consent to marriage and she remains silent, what is the ruling?
A) Her silence is valid consent because she is shy.
B) Her silence is not consent; she must speak explicitly.
C) Her silence is consent only if she laughs.
D) Her silence is consent but the marriage is valid only with a mahr.

Key Takeaway
Silence is only consent when asked by a wali or his messenger; mahr is not a condition for consent.
