Informant Conditions When News of Marriage Reaches a Virgin
Ruling on a virgin's silence upon hearing news of her marriage; dispute on informant conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to STATE the conditions required for an informant (mukhbir) when a virgin hears news of her marriage and remains silent, and DISTINGUISH the positions of Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
A virgin's silence is consent only if the informant meets conditions; Abu Hanifa requires number or justice.

In Hanafi nikah, a virgin's silence is taken as consent, but only if she is properly informed. This lesson examines a dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two disciples regarding the informant's qualifications when he is a third party (fulani). The ruling affects whether her silence amounts to acceptance of the marriage.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the disputed point
The act is the virgin's silence upon hearing news of her marriage. The disputed point is what conditions the informant must meet for her silence to constitute valid consent.
The informant is not the wali or his messenger, but a third person.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position
Abu Hanifa holds that if the informant is a third party, he must be either at least two people, or if one, he must be just (adil).
This is a restrictive condition, analogous to testimony requirements.
3
State the Sahibayn's position
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani do not require any condition; any single informant suffices regardless of number or justice.
Their view is more lenient, prioritizing the likelihood of truth over formal verification.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: Under Abu Hanifa, if a single non-adil person informs, the virgin's silence does not constitute consent; under the Sahibayn, it does.
This difference impacts the validity of the marriage if later contested.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وزوجها فبلغها الخبر فسكتت فهو على ما ذكر نالان وجه الدلالة في السكوت لا يختلف ثم المخبران كان فضوليا يشترط فيه العدد او العدالة عند ابي حنيفة خلافالهما ولو كان رسولا لا يشترط اجماعا وله نظائر
And if he married her and the news reached her and she remained silent, then the ruling is as mentioned, because the aspect of indication in silence does not differ. Then, if the informant was a third party (fulani), it is conditioned upon number or justice according to Abu Hanifa, contrary to the two. And if he was a messenger, it is not conditioned by consensus. And it has parallels.
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of a virgin's consent. If her wali marries her without asking permission, and she later hears the news and remains silent, the same rules apply: silence is consent if the informant is the wali or his messenger, but not if a stranger. The reason is that the evidential value of silence does not change whether before or after marriage. The passage then introduces a dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and the two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) regarding the informant when he is a 'fulani' (a third party not the wali or his messenger). Abu Hanifa requires that the informant be at least two people, or if one, then he must be just (adil). The Sahibayn do not require this condition; a single informant, just or not, suffices. If the informant is the wali's messenger, there is consensus that no such condition applies. This difference has parallels in other areas of fiqh, such as informing about the dismissal of a legal agent (wakil).

Key Terms
المخبر (al-mukhbir): The informant who brings news of the marriage to the virgin.
الفضولي (al-fululi): A third party who is not the wali or his messenger; an unauthorized person.
العدالة (al-adala): Justice; the quality of being upright and trustworthy in legal matters.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
A virgin's silence upon hearing news of her marriage constitutes valid consent only if the informant meets the conditions defined by the Hanafi school — a requirement of number or justice for third parties per Abu Hanifa.

The Obligation of Verbal Consent for a Thayyib
Ruling that a thayyib must consent verbally to marriage

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a thayyib must consent verbally in marriage and identify the textual proof and the rationale behind it.

Core Idea
A thayyib's consent must be spoken; silence is never enough.

This lesson examines a key ruling in Hanafi marriage law: the requirement for a previously married woman (thayyib) to express her consent verbally. We will analyze the hadith proof and the legal reasoning that distinguishes her from a virgin. Understanding this distinction ensures marriages are validly contracted.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is seeking permission from a thayyib. The ruling: her consent is incumbent by speech (not silence).
2
Locate the primary proof
The Prophet ﷺ said: 'The thayyib is consulted' (الثيب تشاور). This hadith is the main textual evidence.
The imperative form 'تشاور' indicates obligation in Hanafi usul.
3
Understand the rationale (ta'lil)
Speech is not considered a flaw for a thayyib, and her shyness is lessened due to marital experience. Thus, there is no impediment to her speaking.
This rationale is additional support beyond the hadith.
4
Contrast with the virgin's case
A virgin's silence can be taken as consent due to shyness. This contrast highlights why verbal consent is required only for the thayyib.
This distinction is unique to Hanafi fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو استاذن الثيب فلابد من رضاها بالقول لقوله عليه السلام الثيب تشاور ولان النطق لا يعد عيبا منها وقل الحياء بالممارسة فلامانع من النطق في حقها
And if permission is sought from a previously married woman (thayyib), then her consent is incumbent by speech, due to his saying (peace be upon him): 'The thayyib is consulted.' And because speech is not considered a flaw for her, and shyness is diminished by practice, so there is no impediment to speech for her.
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage from Al-Hidayah states the ruling for a thayyib—a woman who has been previously married (widowed or divorced). Unlike a virgin, her consent must be expressed verbally; silence is never sufficient. The proof is the Prophetic hadith: 'The thayyib is consulted' (تُشَاوَرُ), which indicates that her opinion must be sought and she must respond verbally. The reason is that for a thayyib, speaking about marriage is not seen as immodest, as her experience has lessened her shyness. Therefore, there is no hindrance to her speaking her consent clearly. This ruling is consistent across the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
الثيب (al-thayyib): A woman who has been previously married (widowed or divorced) and is now marriageable.
تشاور (tushawaru): Is consulted; refers to seeking and obtaining the woman's opinion in marriage matters.
الحياء (al-haya'): Shyness or modesty; the reason why a virgin is allowed to remain silent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because she is no longer shy due to marital experience.
B) Because she is older and wiser.
C) Because the hadith explicitly says so.
D) Because silence is never allowed in marriage.

Key Takeaway
A thayyib's consent to marriage must be given verbally; silence is not acceptable because her shyness is lessened and the hadith commands consultation, which requires speech.

The Silence of a Virgin: Consent and the Conditions of the Informer
Intra-Hanafi dispute on conditions of a stranger informer for a virgin's silence as consent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a virgin's silence as consent, distinguish the conditions when the informer is a stranger according to Abu Hanifa versus the Sahibayn, and identify the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) of this intra-madhhab dispute.

Core Idea
A virgin's silence is consent, but who tells her matters: Abu Hanifa demands reliability from a stranger informer.

In Hanafi marriage law, a virgin's modesty means her silence can indicate consent. But what if the news of marriage comes from a stranger? The Hanafi school itself disagrees: Abu Hanifa insists on two witnesses or one just person, while the Sahibayn accept any informer. This lesson unpacks the reasoning and the practical difference.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and the category of informer
First, understand that a virgin's silence is considered consent (idhn). Then note the three types of informer: the guardian (walī), the guardian's messenger (rasūl), and a stranger (fādūlī).
The ruling changes based on the informer type.
2
Note the consensus
When the informer is the guardian or his messenger, silence suffices without any conditions on the informer.
This is agreed upon (ijmāʿ) within the Hanafi school.
3
Locate the dispute
When the informer is a stranger, a disagreement arises: Abu Hanifa requires either two informers or one just person; the Sahibayn require neither condition.
This is the core of the intra-Hanafi ikhtilāf.
4
Analyse the dalil
Both sides agree on the wajh al-dalāla: silence indicates consent. The dispute centres on the reliability of the source. Abu Hanifa emphasizes caution (ihtiyāt) in marriage rights; the Sahibayn consider the silence itself as sufficient proof regardless of the informer.
The dalīl is aqli based on the principle of caution versus general permissibility.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
If a stranger reports the marriage and the woman remains silent, under Abu Hanifa the marriage is not valid unless the informer meets the conditions; under the Sahibayn it is valid.
The muftā bihī is Abu Hanifa's view.

Translation: And if the guardian married the adult virgin, then the news reached her and she remained silent, then the ruling follows the same detail we mentioned. And among the parallels: if someone dismisses his agent and a stranger reports the dismissal, then according to Abu Hanifa, number or justice is required in the stranger; according to the two companions, no condition. Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the silence of a virgin when she is informed of her marriage by the guardian or another. The ruling states that her silence is considered consent (idhn) because silence indicates consent consistently whether before or after the marriage. However, the nature of the informer matters. If the informer is the guardian himself or his messenger, then silence is sufficient. If the informer is a stranger (fādūlī), then Abu Hanifa requires that the informer be either two people or one just person, while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) do not require any condition regarding number or justice. The Sahibayn maintain that any news, even from one person, is sufficient to establish consent if the woman remains silent. Abu Hanifa's view is more cautious because a stranger's report may be unreliable. The text gives an analogy: if a person dismisses his agent and a stranger reports it, the same conditions apply. The relied-upon position (muftā bihī) in the Hanafi school is Abu Hanifa's view, as it is more protective of the woman's interests. The ruling applies only to a virgin (bikr); a previously married woman (thayyib) requires explicit verbal consent, as covered in the next passage.

Key Terms
فضولی (fādūlī): A person who is neither the guardian (walī) nor the guardian's messenger (rasūl); an unrelated third party who informs the woman of her marriage.
سكوت (sukūt): Silence after being informed, considered as a valid indication of consent for a virgin under certain conditions.

Ruling on Thayyib's Consent: Must Be Verbal
States that a thayyib must give explicit verbal consent for marriage.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the requirement for a thayyib's consent in marriage and explain the evidence from hadith and rational reasoning.

Core Idea
A thayyib must consent verbally; silence does not suffice.

This lesson examines the distinct requirement for a thayyib (previously married woman) regarding consent in marriage. In contrast to a virgin, whose silence can indicate consent due to modesty, the Hanafi school demands explicit verbal expression from a thayyib, based on a prophetic hadith and logical reasoning. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly applying the conditions of a valid marriage contract.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
Extract from the passage the specific ruling: a thayyib must express her consent to marriage by speech (qaul). This is wajib (obligatory).
Note that the ruling contrasts with the default for a virgin.
2
Examine the primary proof
Analyze the hadith 'al-thayyib tushāwar'. The word 'tushāwar' is derived from 'shūrā' (consultation), which requires verbal exchange. Therefore, the hadith mandates speech from the thayyib.
The Hanafi school uses this linguistic meaning to infer wujūb of verbal consent.
3
Analyze the rational secondary proof
The passage gives a rational argument: a thayyib is not as shy as a virgin because she has had prior marital experience; thus, speaking about marriage is not a flaw or embarrassment. Hence, there is no impediment to her speaking.
This reinforces the hadith-based ruling.
4
Contrast with a virgin
Contrast: a virgin may consent by silence because shyness makes speech difficult; her silence is taken as consent. For a thayyib, this reasoning does not apply.
This distinction is a key point in Hanafi nikah fiqh.
5
Confirm the Hanafi position
The Hanafi school is unanimous on this ruling. There is no ikhtilaf among Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad. The mufta bihi is clear.
Ensure you know the authority weight: Zahir al-Riwayah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو استأذن الثيب فلابد من رضاها بالقول لقوله عليه السلام الثيب تشاور ولان النطق لا يعد عيبا منها وقل الحياء بالممارسة فلا مانع من النطق في حقها ترجمہ اور اگر اجازت طلب کی ثیبہ سے تو اس کی رضا مندی قول کے ساتھ ضروری ہے۔ حضور صلى الله عليه وسلم کے فرمان کی وجہ سے کہ نشیبہ سے مشورہ طلب کیا جائے اور اس لئے کہ شیبہ کے حق میں تکلم عیب شمار نہیں کیا جاتا ہے اور حیاء ممارست کی وجہ سے کم ہو گئی پس اس کے حق میں تکلم سے کوئی مانع نہیں ۔ تشریح اگر شیبہ بالغہ سے نکاح کے بارے میں اجازت طلب کی گئی تو اس کی طرف سے رضامندی کا اظہار زبان سے ضروری ہے۔ دلیل حضور صلى الله عليه وسلم کا ارشاد الشیب تشاور - تشاور مشاور مشاورت باب مفاعلت سے ہے اور مفاعلت کا خاصہ ہے اشتراک اور مشورہ کہتے ہیں طلب رائے بالقول کو یعنی زبان سے بول کر رائے طلب کرنا۔ پس جب احد الجانبین میں قول ہے تو جانب آخر میں بھی قول ہوگا۔ پس حدیث کا مقتضی تکلم ہے اور یہی اصل ہے۔ دوسری دلیل عقلی یہ ہے کہ کلام کرنا ثیبہ کے حق میں عیب بھی شمار نہیں کیا جاتا ، اور مردوں کے ساتھ اختلاط کی وجہ سے اس کی حیا بھی کم ہوگئی لہذا اس کے حق میں کلام کرنے سے کوئی مانع نہیں رہا۔
And if permission is sought from the thayyib, then her consent must be by speech, because of the saying of the Prophet (peace be upon him): 'The thayyib is consulted (tushāwar),' and because speech is not considered a flaw from her, and shyness has decreased due to practice (mumārasa), so there is no impediment to speech in her case. Translation: And if permission is sought from a thayyib (previously married woman), then her consent must be by speech. Because of the command of the Prophet (peace be upon him) that the thayyib should be consulted (tushāwar), and because speech is not considered a flaw for her, and her shyness has diminished due to interaction with men, so there is no impediment to her speaking. Explanation: If a thayyib adult woman is asked for permission regarding marriage, she must express consent verbally. The proof is the Prophet's statement 'al-thayyib tushāwar' – consultation is from the root sh-w-r, which implies seeking opinion by speech. Since one side uses speech, the other side must also use speech. Thus, the hadith requires speech. The second rational proof is that speaking is not considered a flaw for a thayyib, and her shyness has decreased due to mixing with men, so there is no impediment.
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage presents the ruling that a thayyib (a woman who has been previously married and has had sexual experience) must give her consent for marriage verbally; silence is not sufficient. This is in contrast to a virgin, whose silence can indicate consent due to modesty. The primary proof is a hadith: 'The thayyib is consulted (tushāwar).' The word 'tushāwar' is derived from 'mushāwara' (consultation), which implies a verbal exchange. Therefore, the hadith requires speech. The second proof is rational: a thayyib is not as shy as a virgin because she has had prior marital experience, so speaking about marriage is not embarrassing for her. Consequently, there is no impediment to her verbal expression. This ruling is unanimous among the Hanafi school; there is no significant ikhtilaf on this point. The muftā bihī position is clear: a thayyib's consent must be by explicit speech (qaul).

Key Terms
ثیبہ (thayyib): A woman who has been previously married and has had sexual intercourse, thus losing her virginity.
تشاور (tushāwar): Derived from shūrā (consultation); it implies seeking opinion verbally, thus requiring speech.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary proof that a thayyib must give verbal consent for marriage?
A) The Quranic verse on marriage.
B) The hadith 'al-thayyib tushāwar'.
C) Consensus of the Sahaba.
D) Rational analogy with virgins.

Key Takeaway
A thayyib's consent for marriage must be explicitly spoken; silence is insufficient.

Hymen Loss and Marriage Consent: Bikr or Thayyib?
Ruling on consent classification after hymen loss

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for consent when a woman's hymen is lost due to non-sexual causes and due to zina, and explain the intra-madhhab dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
Hymen loss from zina does not strip a woman of virginity for consent – silence suffices, per Abu Hanifa.

The Hanafi school distinguishes between causes of hymen loss for the purposes of consent in marriage. While non-sexual causes preserve the ruling of virginity, there is a notable dispute when zina is involved. In this lesson, we will examine the proofs and reasoning behind the position of Imam Abu Hanifa and the opposing view of the Sahibayn, and understand the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) for marriage validity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the undisputed rule
Determine the ruling for hymen loss due to non-sexual causes (jump, menstruation, wound, old age). The text states that the woman remains in the ruling of a virgin (bikr), so her silence suffices for consent.
This is agreed upon by all Hanafi authorities and is from Zahir al-Riwayah.
2
Identify the disputed case
When hymen is lost due to zina, there is a dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). Note the position of Imam al-Shafi'i as well.
Abu Hanifa treats her as bikr; the Sahibayn and Shafi'i treat her as thayyib.
3
Follow each side's linguistic and social reasoning
Examine the linguistic arguments: 'bikr' implies 'first', 'thayyib' implies 'return'. Also consider the social/situational reasoning: people consider her a virgin, so speaking would be a flaw; concealment of zina is recommended.
Abu Hanifa prioritizes social reality over literal fact; Sahibayn focus on actual intercourse.
4
Understand the exclusion of wat' bi-shubha and fāsid nikāh
Note that the dispute only applies to zina, not to intercourse under shubha or invalid marriage, because the Shari'a attaches legal rulings to those, making them apparent. For those, the woman is definitely a thayyib.
This distinction is crucial to avoid overgeneralization.
5
Determine the muftā bihī and thamarah al-khilaf
The Hanafi school follows Abu Hanifa's view as the mufta bihi. The practical consequence: if such a woman remains silent after being informed of marriage, the marriage is valid according to Abu Hanifa, but not according to the Sahibayn until she speaks.
This ruling is more lenient and protects her modesty and interests.

And when her virginity is lost by a jump, or menstruation, or a wound, or old age (ta'nīs), then she is in the ruling of virgins, because she is truly a virgin (bikr), because the one who penetrates her is the first to penetrate her, and from that comes 'bākūrah' (first fruit) and 'bikr' (first), and because she feels shy due to lack of practice (mumārasa). And if her virginity is lost by fornication (zinā), then she is likewise according to Abu Hanifa. But Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, and al-Shafi'i said: Her silence is not sufficient, because she is truly a thayyib, because the one who penetrates her returns to her, and from that comes 'mathūbah' (reward), 'mathābah' (place of return), and 'tathwīb' (calling to prayer). And for Abu Hanifa: People consider her a virgin, so they would find her speech flawed (ma'yūb), so she abstains from speech, so her silence is sufficient so that her interests are not hindered; unlike the case where she is penetrated under a shubha (doubtful intercourse) or an invalid marriage (nikāh fāsid), because the Shari'a has made that apparent, attaching rulings to it. As for zinā, concealment is recommended, so that even if her status becomes notorious, her silence is not relied upon. Translation (Urdu): And when virginity is lost by a jump, menstruation, wound, or old age, she is in the ruling of a virgin, because she is truly a virgin and she feels shy due to lack of practice. And if her virginity is lost by zinā, she is likewise according to Abu Hanifa. But Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, and Imam al-Shafi'i said that her silence is not sufficient, because she is truly a thayyib, because the one who penetrates her returns to her. And the proof of Abu Hanifa is that people consider her a virgin, so they would consider her speech a flaw, so she refrains from speech; therefore, her silence is sufficient so that her interests are not hindered. Unlike the case where she is penetrated by shubha or invalid marriage, because the Shari'a made that apparent. As for zinā, concealment is recommended, so even if her condition becomes notorious, her silence is not relied upon. Explanation: The ruling is that if a girl's virginity is lost due to jumping, excessive menstruation, injury, or staying unmarried for a long time, she is considered a virgin in terms of consent; her silence is consent and speech is not required. The proof is that she is truly a virgin linguistically and lacks experience. If virginity is lost by zinā, Abu Hanifa also considers her a virgin, while the Sahibayn and al-Shafi'i consider her a thayyib, requiring verbal consent. This is because Abu Hanifa considers that people see her as a virgin, so she is reluctant to speak; the Sahibayn consider the actual loss of hymen as the basis.
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage addresses the legal classification of a woman whose hymen is lost due to various causes, specifically for the purpose of consent in marriage. The ruling distinguishes between causes that preserve the ruling of a virgin (bikr) and those that change it to that of a thayyib. If the hymen is lost by a non-sexual cause such as jumping, menstruation, injury, or old age (ta'nīs), the woman is considered a virgin in all respects, because she has never had sexual intercourse. Therefore, her silence is sufficient as consent. This is based on the literal meaning of 'bikr' (first) and the fact that she still has the modesty of a virgin. If the hymen is lost due to zinā (fornication), there is a dispute. Abu Hanifa holds that she remains in the ruling of a virgin because people generally consider her a virgin (since zinā is hidden), and she would be too shy to speak openly; thus, her silence is sufficient. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) and Imam al-Shafi'i hold that she is a thayyib because she has actually experienced intercourse, so she must consent verbally. The Hanafi response is based on the social reality: people consider her a virgin, so speaking would be embarrassing for her, and to protect her interests, silence suffices. However, this applies only to zinā, not to intercourse under shubha or invalid marriage, because those are legally recognized as intercourse and the Shari'a attaches rulings to them. The passage also notes that if her zinā becomes notorious, then even Abu Hanifa may require verbal consent because the concealment is no longer possible. The muftā bihī position in the Hanafi school is the view of Abu Hanifa, as it is more lenient and considerate of women's modesty and interests.

Key Terms
بكر (bikr): A woman who has never had sexual intercourse (virgin). Used here to denote the legal ruling that her silence is consent.
ثیب (thayyib): A woman who has had sexual intercourse, whether legally (marriage) or illegally (zina), according to the Sahibayn. Abu Hanifa excludes zina from this definition for the purpose of consent.
وطی بشبهة (wat' bi-shubha): Sexual intercourse under a mistaken belief that it is lawful (e.g., thinking a woman is one's wife). This is treated as legal intercourse by the Shari'a.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school (mufta bihi), what is the requirement for consent from a woman whose hymen was lost due to zina?
A) She must give explicit verbal consent because she is a thayyib.
B) Her silence is sufficient because she is treated as a virgin.
C) She must have a guardian speak on her behalf.
D) She cannot marry without a judicial decree.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a woman whose hymen is lost due to zina is still considered a virgin for the purpose of consent, so her silence suffices – the muftā bihī position of Abu Hanifa.

Consent Requirement for Women with Hymen Lost by Zina
Dispute over consent for woman with hymen lost by zina

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on the consent requirement for a woman whose hymen is lost due to fornication, and evaluate the opposing view of the Sahibayn and Shafi‘i.

Core Idea
For consent in marriage, Hanafi law treats a woman with hymen lost by zina as a virgin (silence suffices) to prevent social harm.

The consent of a woman in marriage (izn) differs between a virgin (bikr) and a non-virgin (thayyib). A bikr may consent by silence; a thayyib must speak. But what if a woman loses her hymen through fornication? This passage from al-Hidayah examines a key intra-Hanafi and inter-madhhab dispute over whether such a woman is legally a bikr or thayyib, and why Abu Hanifa takes a unique position prioritizing social benefit over linguistic definition.

Guided Path
1
Classify the cases of hymen loss
Distinguish non-sexual causes (jump, menstruation, injury, old age) from sexual causes (zina, shubha, fasid marriage). Note the agreed rulings for each.
Non-sexual causes universally keep her a bikr; shubha and fasid marriage make her thayyib by consensus.
2
State the Hanafi position on zina-loss
Abu Hanifa: she is a bikr, silence suffices. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) and al-Shafi‘i: she is a thayyib, must speak.
3
Examine the opposing proof
The Sahibayn rely on the linguistic root of thayyib (return) and the fact of penetration, which factually makes her a non-virgin.
Root th-y-b implies recurrence; she has experienced the act, so she returns to it.
4
Analyse Abu Hanifa's response
Social perception and prevention of harm (darar) take precedence over literal definition. People see her as a virgin; requiring speech would cause disgrace and obstruct her marriage.
The Law recommends concealing zina, so her private sin should not affect her legal status.
5
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf and exceptions
Practical difference: consent method (silence vs. speech). Exception: if zina is notorious (four witnesses), Abu Hanifa agrees she becomes thayyib. Also, shubha/fasid marriage universally makes her thayyib.
The exception for notorious zina shows that social perception cuts both ways.

ترجمہ اور جب زائل ہو گئی بکارت کو دنے سے یا حیض سے یا زخم سے یا در ازگی عمر سے تو وہ باکرہ کے حکم میں ہے اس لئے کہ وہ حقیقتہ باکرہ ہے اور اس لئے کہ وہ عدم ممارست کی وجہ سے حیاء کرے گی۔ اور اگر اس کی بکارت زنا سے زائل ہوگئی تو وہ ایسی ہی ہے ابوحنیفہ کے نزدیک۔ اور ابو یوسف، امام محمد اور امام شافعی نے فرمایا کہ اس کے سکوت پر اکتفاء نہ کرے اس لئے کہ وہ حقیقتا ثیبہ ہے اس لئے کہ جو اس کو پہنچ گیا وہ اس کو دوبارہ پہنچے گا اور اسی سے مصوبہ اور مثابہ اور تقریب ہے۔ اور ابو حنیفہ کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ لوگ اسکو باکرہ سمجھتے ہیں پس نطق سے اس کو معیوب سمجھیں گے پھر وہ نطق سے رکے گی اس لئے سکوت پر اکتفاء کرے تا کہ اس پر اس کے مصالح معطل نہ ہو جائیں، بخلاف اس صورت کے کہ جب اس سے وطی بشبتہ کی گئی یا نکاح فاسد سے اس لئے کہ شریعت نے اس کو ظاہر کر دیا چنانچہ اس پر احکام معلق کئے ہیں بہر حال زنا تو مندوب اس کا چھپانا ہے، حتی کہ اگر اس کا حال یعنی زنا کرنا مشتہر ہو جائے تو اس کے سکوت پر اکتفاء کرے۔
تشریح مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ اگر کسی لڑکی کی بکارت زائل ہو گئی کو دنے کی وجہ سے یا کثرت حیض کی وجہ سے یا کسی زنم کی وجہ سے یا زیادہ مدت ٹھہرنے کی وجہ سے تو ان سب صورتوں میں یہ لڑکی باکرہ کے حکم میں ہوگی یعنی استیذان کے وقت اس کا سکوت اذن ہو گا کلام کرنا ضروری نہیں۔ دلیل یہ ہے کہ یہ عورت حقیقت میں باکرہ ہے اور با کاف، را، کے مادہ میں اولیت کے معنی پائے جاتے ہیں۔ جیسے باکورہ پہلا پھل اور بکرہ اول صبح کو کہتے ہیں پس اس عورت کے پاس جو بھی پہنچے گا وہ پہلا ہی پہنچنے والا ہو گا۔ لہذا اس عورت کو باکرہ ہی کا حکم دیا جائے گا۔ دوسری دلیل یہ ہے کہ اس عورت کو مردوں کیساتھ اختلاط اور ممارست کا موقع تو ملا نہیں لہذا یہ بھی حیا اور شرم کے باعث زبان سے اجازت نہ دے گی ۔ اور اگر باکرہ کی بکارت زنا سے زائل ہوگئی ہے تو امام ابوحنیفہ کے نزدیک یہ بھی باکرہ کے حکم میں ہے۔ اور صاحبین اور امام شافعی کے نزدیک ثیبہ کے حکم میں ہے، لہذا اس کے سکوت پر اکتفاء نہیں کیا جائے گا بلکہ زبان سے اجازت دینا ضروری ہوگا۔ ان حضرات کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ ث ، یا ب کے مادہ میں عود کے معنی ہیں مثلاً مٹوبہ نیک عمل کی جزاء کو کہتے ہیں تو اب گویا دوبارہ عمل مل گیا ایسا ہی مشابه بار بارلوٹ کر آنے کی جگہ ، اسی وجہ سے مکہ کو مثابة کہتے ہیں کہ لوگ بار بار حج اور عمرہ کیلئے لوٹ کر جاتے ہیں اسی طرح تشویب اعلام بعد الا سلام کو کہتے ہیں۔ حاصل دلیل یہ ہوا کہ جس عورت کی بکارت زنا سے زائل ہو گئی وہ حقیقت میں شیبہ ہے کیونکہ جو اس کو ایک مرتبہ پہنچ گیا دوبارہ بھی وہی فعل ہوا۔ پس جب اس عورت کے ساتھ دوبارہ بھی وہی فعل واقع ہوگا جو ایک مرتبہ زنا سے ہوا تو وہ شیبہ ہے ،لہذا اس پر شیبہ ہی کے احکام لگائے جائیں گے۔
امام ابو حنیفہ کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ لوگ اس عورت کو باکرہ سمجھتے ہیں اب اگر کلام کرے گی تو لوگ اس کو معیوب قرار دیں گے اور یہ اس سے یہ حیا کرے گی اور کلام کرنے سے رکے گی ، اس کے نتیجہ میں اس کے مصالح معطل ہو کر رہ جائیں گے اس لئے اس کو باکرہ ہی کے حکم میں رکھا جائے۔ البتہ اگر باکرہ سے وطی بشبہ کی گئی یا نکاح فاسد سے وطی کی گئی تو یہ بالا تفاق ثیبہ ہوگی اس لئے کہ شریعت نے اس پر ثیبہ کے احکام معلق کر کے اس کو ظاہر کر دیا چنانچہ عدت واجب ہو گی مہر واجب ہوگا ، اور رہا زنا تو اس میں چھپانا مستحب ہے حتی کہ اگر اس کا حال مشتہر ہو گیا اور زنا پر چار گواہوں نے گواہی دے دی تو پھر یہ بھی ثیبہ کے حکم میں ہوگی اور اس کے سکوت پر اکتفا نہیں کیا جائے گا۔ واللہ اعلم ۔
And when her virginity is removed by a jump, or menstruation, or a wound, or old age, then she is in the ruling of virgins, because she is a virgin in truth: the one who penetrates her is the first to penetrate her, hence the words 'al-bakurah' (first fruit) and 'al-bukrah' (dawn). And because she is shy due to lack of practice. And if her virginity is removed by fornication, then she is similarly (in the ruling of a virgin) according to Abu Hanifa. Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, and al-Shafi'i said: Her silence is not sufficient, because she is truly a non-virgin: the one who penetrates her returns to her, hence the words 'al-mathubah' (reward), 'al-mathabah' (place of return), and 'al-tathwib' (repetition). And for Abu Hanifa, the people know her as a virgin, so they would consider her defective if she speaks, so she refrains from speaking; thus her silence is sufficient so that her interests are not thwarted. Unlike if she was penetrated by a shubha (uncertain) intercourse or a void marriage, because the Law has made it apparent by attaching rulings to it. As for fornication, covering it is recommended, unless her condition becomes notorious, then her silence is not sufficient.
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage addresses the legal classification of a woman for consent in marriage (izn). The Hanafi school distinguishes between factual virginity and legal virginity for the purpose of consent. If the hymen is lost without sexual intercourse (jumping, menstruation, injury, old age), she is treated as a virgin (bikr) legally: her silence suffices as consent. This is based on the literal meaning of 'bikr' as first penetration, and her modesty prevents her from speaking. The dispute arises when the hymen is lost through fornication (zina). Abu Hanifa holds that she is still legally a virgin for consent, because society perceives her as a virgin and requiring her to speak would cause her harm (defamation). The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) and al-Shafi'i argue that she is truly a non-virgin (thayyib) because she has experienced penetration, and therefore her silence is insufficient; she must explicitly consent verbally. The passage further clarifies that if the hymen is lost due to shubha (mistaken intercourse) or a fasid marriage, all agree she becomes a non-virgin, because Islamic law attaches legal consequences (iddah, mahr). Fornication is recommended to be concealed, so it does not have the same effect unless the matter becomes publicly known through testimony of four witnesses.

Key Terms
باكرة (bākiratun): A woman who has never had lawful or unlawful sexual intercourse; legally, one whose silence suffices for consent in marriage.
ثيب (thayyib): A woman who has had lawful or unlawful sexual intercourse; legally, one who must explicitly consent to marriage verbally.
سكوت (sukūt): Silence; for a virgin, silence is deemed consent (izn) in marriage; for a thayyib, explicit speech is required.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which of the following cases makes a woman a thayyib (non-virgin) for marriage consent?
A) Loss of hymen by jumping.
B) Loss of hymen by fornication (zina) under Abu Hanifa.
C) Loss of hymen by shubha (mistaken intercourse) by consensus.
D) Loss of hymen by menstruation.

Key Takeaway
A woman's legal status for marriage consent depends on the cause of hymen loss: non-sexual causes keep her a virgin; zina is disputed, but the Hanafi mufta bihi treats her as a virgin to prevent social harm.

Credibility in Marriage Proposal Disputes: Silence vs Rejection
Intra-Hanafi dispute over credibility in marriage proposal silence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on whose statement is accepted when a wife claims rejection and the husband claims silence, explain the analogy to a depositary (wadi'), and distinguish the case from Zufar's analogy to khiyar.

Core Idea
In marriage disputes, the wife's denial of consent is believed because she is the defendant; silence does not create a binding contract.

When a marriage proposal is made, the husband may claim that the wife remained silent (implying consent), but the wife may claim she verbally rejected the proposal. Whose statement is accepted in court? This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Hanifa and the two Sahibayn rule that the wife's statement is accepted, while Imam Zufar disagrees, analogizing silence to a binding contract after an option period. We will examine the reasoning behind each position and the Hanafi preference for the wife's credibility.

Guided Path
1
Identify the competing claims
The husband claims the marriage is established because the wife was silent (consent). The wife claims she rejected the proposal. Each party's claim leads to different legal consequences.
The husband asserts luzum (binding) and ownership of intimacy (tamalluk al-bid').
2
Understand the Hanafi default: defendant's statement is accepted
The wife is the defendant (munkirah) because she denies the husband's claim. The principle is that the defendant's statement is accepted if the plaintiff has no evidence. This is analogous to a depositary (wadi') who denies the return of a deposit.
The analogy: Just as a depositary's denial is believed, the wife's denial of consent is believed.
3
Examine Zufar's qiyas to khiyar
Zufar argues that silence is the original state (asl) and rejection is an exception ('arid). He analogizes to a buyer with a stipulated option (khiyar shart) who claims rejection after the option period: the contract is binding and the rejection claim is not accepted.
Zufar's analogy implies that silence, like the passage of time in khiyar, establishes the contract.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response distinguishing khiyar
The Hanafi scholars respond that in khiyar, the contract becomes binding (lazim) once the option period passes, so a claim of rejection contradicts the established binding. In marriage, no such binding exists until consent is confirmed. Therefore, the wife's denial is valid as she is defending her right to reject.
The key distinction: luzum is already established in khiyar, but not in the case of silence.
5
Recognize the role of evidence (bayyinah)
If the husband produces witnesses (bayyinah) that the wife was silent, the marriage is established. If not, the wife's statement is accepted, and no oath is required from her, following the principle of al-istikhalf in the six matters.
This is an exception to the general rule that a defendant may be required to take an oath.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مرد نے کہا: جب پیغام نکاح پہنچا تو خاموش رہتی عورت کہتی ہے میں نے رد کر دیا تھا ۔ عورت کا قول معتبر ہے
واذا قال الزوج بلغک النکاح فسكت وقالت رددت فالقول قولها وقال زفر القول قوله لان السكوت اصل والرد عارض فصار كالمشروط له الخيار اذا ادعى الرد بعد مضي المدة ونحن نقول انه يدعى لزوم العقد وتملك البضع والمراة تدفعه فكانت منكرة كالمودع اذا ادعى رد الوديعة بخلاف مسألة الخيار لان اللزوم قد ظهر بمضى المدة وان اقام الزوج البيئة على سكوتها ثبت النكاح لانه نور دعواه بالحجة وان لم تكن له بينة فلا يمين عليها عند ابي حنيفة وهي مسألة الاستخلاف في الاشياء الستة وسيأتیك في الدعوى ان شاء الله
ترجمہ اور جب کہا شوہر نے کہ تجھ کو نکاح کی خبر پہنچی لیکن تو خاموش رہی اور عورت نے کہا میں نے رد کر دیا تھا تو قول عورت ہی کا معتبر ہوگا۔ اور امام زفر نے کہا کہ مرد کا قول معتبر ہو گا اس لئے کہ سکوت اصل ہے اور ر د عارض ، پس ایسا ہو گیا جیسا کہ مشروطلہ الخیار نے جب دعوی کیا اس نے رڈ کا مدت خیار گذر جانے کے بعد اور ہم کہیں گے کہ زوج دعوی کرتا ہے لزوم عقد اور بضع کے مالک ہونے کا اور عورت اس کو دفع کرتی ہے پس عورت منکرہ ہو گئی جیسا کہ مودع جب دعوی کرے ردود اعت کا بخلاف مسئلہ خیار کے اس لئے کہ لزوم مدت کے گذر جانے سے ظاہر ہو گیا۔ اور اگر شوہر نے بینہ قائم کر دیا عورت کی خاموشی پر تو نکاح ثابت ہو جائے گا۔ اس لئے کہ مرد نے اپنے دعوئی کو دلیل سے ثابت کر
The man said: When the marriage proposal reached you, you remained silent. The woman says: I had rejected. The woman's statement is accepted. And when the husband says: 'The marriage proposal reached you and you were silent,' and she says: 'I rejected,' then her statement is accepted. Zufar said: His statement is accepted because silence is the original state, and rejection is an exception; it becomes like the one with a condition of option who claims rejection after the option period has passed. And we say: He claims the binding of the contract and the ownership of intimacy, and the woman repels that, so she is a denier, like the depositary when he claims return of the deposit. Unlike the case of option, because the binding becomes apparent by the passage of the option period. And if the husband produces evidence of her silence, the marriage is established, because he supports his claim with proof. And if he has no evidence, then no oath is required from her according to Abu Hanifa—this is the issue of 'al-istikhalf' concerning the six things, and it will come to you in the chapter of claims (al-da'awa), God willing.
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage deals with a procedural conflict in marriage formation. After a proposal (khitbah), the husband claims the wife was silent (hence consent), but the wife claims she verbally rejected the proposal. The Hanafi position, as given here, is that the wife's statement is believed because she is denying the formation of the marriage contract. She is akin to a defendant denying a claim. Imam Zufar, a Hanafi scholar, disagrees, arguing that silence is the original state (asl) and rejection is an exception ('arid), so the husband's claim should prevail, similar to someone with a khiyar option who claims rejection after the period expires—once the period passes, the option is deemed waived. The Hanafi response refutes this by distinguishing the cases: in the option case, the contract is already binding after the period passes (luzum is established), so the claim of rejection is contrary to the apparent state. Here, there is no luzum until consent is established. The wife's denial is like a depositary (wadi') denying liability after the depositor claims return. Furthermore, if the husband can produce witnesses (bayyinah) to prove her silence, the marriage is established. If he cannot, she is not required to take an oath according to Abu Hanifa—this is related to a separate principle about six matters (al-ashya' al-sittah) where no oath is administered to the defendant, which will be elaborated later in the Book of Claims.

Key Terms
الاستخلاف في الاشياء الستة (al-istikhālaf fī al-ashyā' al-sittah): A principle that in six specific types of claims, the defendant does not take an oath. One of these is the claim of return of a deposit (al-wadi'ah). The wife's denial of consent is analogized to this.
مشروطلہ الخیار (mashrūṭuhu al-khiyār): A person who has a stipulated option (khiyar shart) in a sale. If the option period passes, the contract becomes binding. Zufar analogizes the wife's silence to this.
بينة (bayyinah): Proof, typically two male witnesses (or one male and two females) who testify to the fact. Here, if the husband produces bayyinah on her silence, the marriage is proven.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, if a woman claims she verbally rejected a marriage proposal while the husband claims she was silent, whose statement is accepted?
A) The husband's, because silence is the original state.
B) The wife's, because she is denying the claim of the husband.
C) Neither, until witnesses are produced.
D) Both are rejected.

Key Takeaway
In marriage formation disputes, the wife's denial of consent is believed unless the husband proves her silence with evidence.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute: Virginity Status After Fornication
Intra-Hanafi dispute on bikr vs thayyib after fornication

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding the legal status of a woman who has lost her virginity through fornication, and apply the mufta bihi ruling to a marriage consent scenario.

Core Idea
Fornication does not legally deflower a woman; she remains bikr unless four witnesses testify.

This passage from al-Hidayah presents an important intra-Hanafi dispute. While the Sahibayn argue that any act of intercourse makes a woman thayyib based on linguistic reasoning, Imam Abu Hanifa considers societal custom and modesty, ruling that a woman who lost her virginity through fornication is still treated as bikr in legal matters. Understanding this difference is crucial for correctly determining marriage consent rules.

Guided Path
1
State the dispute
Identify the two conflicting positions: Abu Hanifa says bikr, the Sahibayn say thayyib. Note the practical consequence: bikr's silence suffices, thayyib must speak.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf; the mufta bihi is Abu Hanifa's view.
2
Examine the Sahibayn's linguistic argument
They derive thayyib from the root th-w-b (return). Since the act of intercourse occurred once, it can occur again, so she is thayyib.
Linguistic derivation (ishtiqaq) is a common usul tool.
3
Analyse Abu Hanifa's counter-argument
He prioritizes custom (‘urf) and modesty (haya): people consider her a virgin, and forcing her to speak would disgrace her and harm her interests.
This shows consideration of maqasid (preserving dignity).
4
Identify the consensus exception
If virginity is lost through wati' bi-shubha or nikah fasid, she becomes thayyib by consensus, because Shari'ah explicitly attaches thayyib rulings (e.g., ‘iddah, mahr).
The distinction hinges on the nature of the intercourse: lawful (shubha) vs. unlawful (zina).
5
Apply the mufta bihi
The relied-upon view is Abu Hanifa's: she remains bikr unless fornication is proven by four witnesses. In marriage, her silence suffices as consent.
This ruling is followed by later Hanafi scholars for practical ease.

Source Text

اشرف البدایہ شرح اردو ہدایہ جلد چهارم كتاب النكاح دلیل یہ ہے کہ ث ، یا ب کے مادہ میں عود کے معنی ہیں مثلاً مٹوبہ نیک عمل کی جزاء کو کہتے ہیں تو اب گویا دوبارہ عمل مل گیا ایسا ہی مشابه بار بارلوٹ کر آنے کی جگہ ، اسی وجہ سے مکہ کو مثابة کہتے ہیں کہ لوگ بار بار حج اور عمرہ کیلئے لوٹ کر جاتے ہیں اسی طرح تشویب اعلام بعد الا سلام کو کہتے ہیں۔ حاصل دلیل یہ ہوا کہ جس عورت کی بکارت زنا سے زائل ہو گئی وہ حقیقت میں شیبہ ہے کیونکہ جو اس کو ایک مرتبہ پہنچ گیا دوبارہ بھی وہی فعل ہوا۔ پس جب اس عورت کے ساتھ دوبارہ بھی وہی فعل واقع ہوگا جو ایک مرتبہ زنا سے ہوا تو وہ شیبہ ہے ،لہذا اس پر شیبہ ہی کے احکام لگائے جائیں گے۔
امام ابو حنیفہ کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ لوگ اس عورت کو باکرہ سمجھتے ہیں اب اگر کلام کرے گی تو لوگ اس کو معیوب قرار دیں گے اور یہ اس سے وہ حیا کرے گی اور کلام کرنے سے رکے گی ، اس کے نتیجہ میں اس کے مصالح معطل ہو کر رہ جائیں گے اس لئے اس کو باکرہ ہی کے حکم میں رکھا جائے۔ البتہ اگر باکرہ سے وطی بشبہ کی گئی یا نکاح فاسد سے وطی کی گئی تو یہ بالا تفاق ثیبہ ہوگی اس لئے کہ شریعت نے اس پر ثیبہ کے احکام معلق کر کے اس کو ظاہر کر دیا چنانچہ عدت واجب ہو گی مہر واجب ہوگا ، اور رہا زنا تو اس میں چھپانا مستحب ہے حتی کہ اگر اس کا حال مشتہر ہو گیا اور زنا پر چار گواہوں نے گواہی دے دی تو پھر یہ بھی ثیبہ کے حکم میں ہوگی اور اس کے سکوت پر اکتفا نہیں کیا جائے گا۔ واللہ اعلم
This is the proof: the root th-w-b has the meaning of return, for example 'mathubah' means reward of a good deed, so it is as if the action is repeated. Similarly, a place where people repeatedly return is called 'mathabah', for this reason Makkah is called 'mathabah' because people repeatedly return for Hajj and Umrah. Similarly, 'thayyib' refers to a woman after [first] intercourse. The gist of the proof: a woman whose virginity is removed by fornication is in reality thayyib, because that which reached her once has occurred again. So when that same act occurs with her again as happened once by fornication, she is thayyib, therefore the rulings of thayyib will apply to her. Imam Abu Hanifa's proof is that people consider this woman a virgin; if she speaks, people will consider her defective, and she will be shy and refrain from speaking; consequently her interests will be lost. Therefore she is kept in the ruling of a virgin. However, if a virgin is penetrated by wati' bi-shubha (dubious intercourse) or by a void marriage (nikah fasid), then by consensus she becomes thayyib, because the Shari'ah has made apparent the rulings of thayyib on her, such that 'iddah becomes obligatory, and mahr becomes obligatory. As for fornication, concealing it is recommended; even if her condition becomes publicly known and four witnesses testify to fornication, then she too is in the ruling of thayyib, and her silence will not be accepted. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage discusses an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning a woman who loses her virginity through fornication. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) argue that she should be considered thayyib (deflowered) based on the linguistic derivation of the word thayyib, which implies a return to an act. Since the act of intercourse occurred once, it is as if it has returned. Therefore, the rulings of a thayyib apply. Imam Abu Hanifa, however, argues that such a woman is regarded by society as a virgin (bikr). If she were to speak about this, she would be disgraced and would feel shame, causing her interests to be harmed. Therefore, she retains the legal status of a virgin. There is an exception: if virginity is lost through wati' bi-shubha (intercourse under a false belief of marriage) or through a valid but void marriage (nikah fasid), the woman becomes thayyib by consensus of the school, because the Shari'ah has explicitly attached thayyib rulings to such cases (e.g., 'iddah and mahr become obligatory). The passage also notes that if fornication becomes widely known and four witnesses testify, then she is also treated as thayyib, and her silence is not sufficient. This dispute has practical implications for marriage consent and waiting periods.

Key Terms
ثَيِّب (thayyib): A woman who has had sexual intercourse after being a virgin (bikr), whether through valid marriage, fornication, or dubious intercourse.
بِكْر (bikr): A woman who has never had sexual intercourse.
وطء بشبهة (wati' bi-shubha): Sexual intercourse with a woman under a mistaken belief that it is lawful (e.g., marrying without proper witnesses or thinking a woman is his wife).
نكاح فاسد (nikah fasid): A marriage contract that is defective in its conditions (e.g., without witnesses) but not invalid per se.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, a woman whose hymen is broken by fornication is considered:
A) Thayyib under all circumstances
B) Bikr unless the fornication is proven by four witnesses
C) Bikr unless she was married before
D) Thayyib only if she committed zina multiple times

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, a woman who loses her virginity through fornication retains the legal status of bikr unless proven by four witnesses, due to considerations of custom and modesty.

Nikah — Marriage
intermediate
8 min
Dispute Over Silence After Marriage Proposal
Intra-Hanafi dispute with qiyas arguments

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on the evidentiary dispute over silence in marriage and evaluate the qiyas arguments of Abu Hanifa and Zufar.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa accepts the woman's word without oath; Zufar says the man is believed by analogy to khiyar al-shart.

This lesson examines a legal dispute within the Hanafi school on a procedural issue in marriage: when a man claims a woman was silent upon receiving a proposal (implying consent under Hanafi law) and she claims she rejected, whose statement is accepted in court? Abu Hanifa and Zufar both use qiyas but reach opposite conclusions. The case also illustrates the unique 'six issues' (al-ashya' al-sittah) where Abu Hanifa dispenses with the defendant's oath.

Guided Path
1
Identify the factual scenario and the legal question
Understand that the dispute is about a marriage proposal: the man claims the woman was silent (which constitutes consent for a virgin), the woman claims she explicitly rejected. Both lack witnesses. The question: whose word is accepted in court?
This is a procedural issue (qada) not a substantive rule of marriage.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position and his analogy
Abu Hanifa says the woman's statement is accepted without oath. She is the defendant (mudda'a 'alayh) because her claim aligns with the default (no marriage). The man is the claimant and must bring evidence. This is analogous to a depositor (mudi') who claims to have returned the deposit; his statement is accepted because he denies the claim of continued liability.
The default (asl) is that no marriage exists.
3
State Zufar's position and his analogy
Zufar says the man's statement is accepted. He argues silence is the original state (asl) and rejection is an exception ('arid). He analogizes to khiyar al-shart: after the option period expires, if the option-holder claims rejection, the other party's claim of silence (affirming the contract) is accepted because silence is the original. Similarly here, the man claims silence (original), so his word prevails.
Zufar's qiyas treats the situation as a dispute about whether an exception (rejection) occurred.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response distinguishing the analogies
The Hanafi response rejects Zufar's analogy: in khiyar, the contract becomes binding automatically after the period, regardless of any claim. The binding is not based on belief in one party's statement. In our case, the very existence of the marriage is disputed, so the default (no marriage) governs. The woman is like a depositor denying liability, not like an option-holder bound by time.
The distinction turns on whether the original state (asl) is a fact (no marriage) or a claim (silence).
5
Note the connection to the six issues and the intra-Hanafi difference
Abu Hanifa classifies this case as one of the six issues where the defendant's oath is not required. The Sahibayn and other schools require an oath. This highlights an internal Hanafi difference on procedure, but the mufta bihi follows Abu Hanifa in this specific case.
The six issues are: riqq, wala', nikah, raj'ah, ila', istila'.

And when the husband says, 'The marriage proposal reached you and you were silent,' and she says, 'I rejected it,' then her statement is accepted. Zufar said: his statement is accepted because silence is the original state and rejection is an exception, so it is like the one who has the option of condition when he claims rejection after the period has passed. But we say: he claims the binding of the contract and ownership of the right of intercourse, and the woman denies it, so she is a denier, like the depositor when he claims return of the deposit. Unlike the case of option because binding becomes apparent after the period. And if the husband brings evidence of her silence, the marriage is established because he supports his claim with proof. If he has no evidence, then no oath is upon her according to Abu Hanifa, and this is one of the six issues of asking for oath, and it will come in the book of Da'wa, God willing. 
Translation: And when the husband said that the news of marriage reached you and you were silent, and the woman said I rejected it, then the woman's statement is accepted. Imam Zufar said that the man's statement will be accepted because silence is the original and rejection is an exception, so it became like the one who has the option when he claims rejection after the expiry of the period. And we say that the husband claims the binding of the contract and ownership of the sexual right, and the woman repels it, so she became a denier, like the depositor when he claims the return of the deposit. Unlike the case of option because binding became apparent after the expiry of the period. And if the husband establishes evidence on her silence, the marriage is proven because he illuminated his claim with proof. And if he does not have evidence, then no oath is upon her according to Imam Abu Hanifa. And this is one of the six issues of asking for oath, and it will come in the book of Da'wa, God willing. Explanation: The scenario is that a man said to a woman: When the news of marriage reached you, you remained silent, therefore the marriage became binding on you. The woman said: As soon as the news reached, I rejected it, so the marriage did not take place. And witnesses are not present with either. According to us, the woman's statement is accepted, and according to Imam Zufar, the man's statement will be accepted. Before the arguments of both parties, understand the definition of claimant (mudda'i) and defendant (mudda'a 'alayh) and their ruling. The claimant is one whose statement is contrary to the original; the defendant is one whose statement is in accordance with the original. According to the hadith, the claimant must provide evidence. If the claimant has no evidence, then the defendant's statement with an oath is accepted. However, there are six things in which, according to Abu Hanifa, the defendant does not take an oath. And according to the Sahibayn, Imam Shafi'i, Imam Malik, and Imam Ahmad, the oath will be taken from the defendant. Those six things are: slavery (riqq), clientage (wala'), marriage (nikah), revocation (raj'ah), vow of abstention (ila'), and conquest (istila'). Now listen to Imam Zufar's argument: He says that silence is the original because silence is the absence of speech, and in everything, absence is the original, and rejection is an exception, i.e., against the original. So the man is the defendant and the woman is the claimant. And the claimant does not have witnesses. Therefore, the defendant's statement, i.e., the husband's, will be accepted. This is like a person who bought a horse from someone and the seller has a three-day option. He may break the sale contract within three days or keep it. After three days, the buyer says: You remained silent during the option period, and the seller says: I rejected it. In this issue, the defendant who claims silence, his statement is accepted because silence is the original and rejection is an exception. Similarly, in the issue of the text, the one claiming silence, i.e., the husband, his statement will be accepted. The summary of our argument is that the man claims that the marriage contract has become binding and he has become owner of the sexual right, and the woman denies it. The original is no marriage and no ownership of the sexual right. So the man's statement is against the original, and the woman's is in accordance with the original. The one whose statement is against the original is the claimant, and the one whose statement is in accordance is the defendant. The claimant (the man) has no witnesses, so the defendant's (the woman's) statement is accepted. This is like the depositor (who had a trust) says: I have returned the deposit, and the owner of the deposit says: you have not returned it. Here, the depositor's statement is accepted because the depositor claims freedom from liability, and the owner claims liability. Freedom from liability is the original, and liability is against the original. So the depositor is the defendant, and the owner is the claimant. The owner has no evidence, so the defendant's statement is accepted. The answer to Imam Zufar's analogy is that in the case of conditional option, the binding of the sale contract is not because the one claiming silence is believed, but because after the expiry of the option period, the contract becomes automatically binding. And in this issue, if the husband presents evidence of the woman's silence, the marriage is established because the claimant has illuminated his claim with proof. And if he has no witnesses, then according to Imam Abu Hanifa, no oath is upon the woman. And this is one of those six things in which, according to Imam Abu Hanifa, the defendant does not take an oath, while others take it. This I have mentioned in the introduction.
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This lesson presents an intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Zufar regarding a procedural question in marriage law. The scenario: a man claims he sent a marriage proposal and the woman remained silent (which for a virgin constitutes consent in Hanafi law), while the woman claims she verbally rejected it immediately. Since both lack witnesses, the question is whose statement is accepted in court. Imam Abu Hanifa rules that the woman's statement is accepted without an oath. She is the defendant (mudda'a 'alayh) because she denies the marriage, while the man is the claimant (mudda'i) asserting it. The default is that no marriage exists, so her claim aligns with the original state. As per the hadith, the claimant must bring evidence; lacking it, the defendant's word is taken without an oath in this case. Imam Zufar argues by analogy to khiyar al-shart (option of condition in sales): If a buyer and seller have an option period, after it expires, if the seller claims the buyer rejected, the buyer's claim of silence (affirming the contract) is accepted because silence is the original state. Similarly here, the man claiming her silence should prevail. The Hanafi response distinguishes that in khiyar, the contract becomes binding automatically after the period, regardless of anyone's claim. The binding is not based on belief in one party's statement. In the marriage case, the very existence of the marriage is disputed, so the principles of evidence apply. The woman is analogous to a depositor who claims to have returned the deposit: he is the defendant denying the claim of continued liability. The lesson also introduces the concept of 'al-ashya' al-sittah' (the six issues) where Abu Hanifa deviates from the normal rule that the defendant must take an oath. These six are: nikah (marriage), raj'ah (revocation of divorce), 'itq (emancipation), wala' (clientage), istila' (conquest of a slave), and something else (riqq or ila'? The text list varies). According to Abu Hanifa, in these cases, the defendant's word is accepted without oath. Other Hanafis (Sahibayn) and other schools require an oath. This specific case (dispute over silence in marriage) is one of these six.

Key Terms
سكوت (sukut): Silence in response to a marriage proposal, which in Hanafi law constitutes consent for a virgin (bikr).
مدعٍ (mudda'i): The claimant, whose statement contradicts the default presumption (asl). He must bring evidence (bayyinah).
مدعى عليه (mudda'a 'alayh): The defendant, whose statement aligns with the default presumption. He takes an oath if the claimant has no evidence, except in six cases (al-ashya' al-sittah).
الاشياء الستة (al-ashya' al-sittah): Six specific legal issues where Abu Hanifa does not require the defendant to take an oath: nikah, raj'ah, 'itq, wala', istila', and something else (riqq or ila'?). The text lists: رق (slavery), ولاء (clientage), نکاح (marriage), رجعت (revocation), ایلاء (vow of abstention), استیلاء (conquest).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, when a man claims a woman was silent after receiving a marriage proposal and she claims she rejected, what is the ruling?
A) The man's statement is accepted because silence is the norm.
B) The woman's statement is accepted without oath.
C) The woman's statement is accepted but she must take an oath.
D) The court investigates and decides based on evidence.

Key Takeaway
In a dispute over silence after a marriage proposal, the Hanafi school (following Abu Hanifa) accepts the woman's statement without oath, as she is the defendant denying the claim of marriage.

The Authority of a Woman's Denial in a Marriage Claim Without Witnesses
Intra-Hanafi dispute on probative value of silence in marriage claims

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rule for determining claimant and defendant in a marriage claim based on silence and apply it to conclude whose statement is accepted without witnesses.

Core Idea
In marriage claims without witnesses, the woman's denial is accepted because non-marriage is the default state.

This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Zufar over a procedural rule: in a marriage claim without witnesses, whose word is accepted? The answer hinges on identifying the original state (asl) and determining who is the plaintiff and who is the defendant. Abu Hanifa's view, which is the relied-upon position, holds that the woman's denial is sufficient and she is not required to take an oath. Understanding this case clarifies the Hanafi method of applying the principle of asl in procedural law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the factual scenario
Read the scenario: a man claims marriage based on the woman's silence; she denies; no witnesses. Recognize that the dispute is about whose statement is accepted and whether an oath is required.
The man claims the woman's silence implies consent; the woman claims she rejected.
2
Apply the definitions of mudda'i and mudda'a alayh
Recall: mudda'i is one whose statement contradicts the original state (asl); mudda'a alayh is one whose statement agrees with the asl. Determine the relevant asl in this case.
The asl is the absence of marriage (no marriage contract proven).
3
Determine the asl and classify the parties
The man claims marriage (contrary to asl) → he is the claimant. The woman denies marriage (conforms to asl) → she is the defendant. Since the claimant has no evidence, the defendant's statement is normally accepted with an oath.
Abu Hanifa makes an exception: this is one of six cases where the defendant is not required to take an oath.
4
Evaluate the analogy to the sale option
Zufar argues that silence is the asl, making the man the defendant. He analogizes to a sale with an option period where the buyer's silence after expiry binds the contract. Analyze why this analogy fails.
In the sale case, the contract becomes binding by the passage of time, not by the acceptance of a claim about silence.
5
Recognize the exception of istikhlaf
Abu Hanifa's position relies on the istikhlaf – a set of six cases where the defendant is not put to oath. Marriage is one of these. Confirm that the woman's denial is accepted without oath.
The other schools (Sahibayn, Shafi'i, Malik, Ahmad) require an oath from the defendant in these cases.

He (the author) said: And if the man does not have evidence, then the woman is not required to take an oath according to Imam Abu Hanifa. And this issue is from the six cases of istikhlaf, and it will come soon in the Book of Claims, God willing. Explanation of the scenario: A man said to a woman: When you received news of the marriage, you remained silent, so the marriage became binding on you. The woman said: When I was informed, I rejected (the marriage), so the marriage did not occur. And there are no witnesses on either side. According to us, the woman's statement is accepted, and according to Imam Zufar, the man's statement is accepted. Before presenting the arguments of both sides, understand the definition of the claimant (mudda'i) and the defendant (mudda'a alayh) and their ruling. The claimant is one whose statement is contrary to the original state (asl), and the defendant is one whose statement is in conformity with the original state. According to the hadith, the burden of proof (bayyinah) is on the claimant. If the claimant does not have evidence, then the defendant's statement accompanied by an oath is accepted. However, there are six cases in which, according to Abu Hanifa, the defendant is not required to take an oath. And the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), Imam Shafi'i, Imam Malik, and Imam Ahmad hold that an oath should be taken from the defendant in these six cases. The six cases are: slavery (riqq), clientage (wala'), this marriage (al-nikah), return (ruju'?), oath for ila? (fi fi ila'), seizure (istila'?) Now listen to Imam Zufar's argument: He says that silence is the original state (asl) because silence is the absence of speech, and in all things non-existence is the original state, and rejection is an accident (a change from the original). So the man is the defendant (mudda'a alayh) and the woman is the claimant (mudda'iyah). And the claimant does not have witnesses. Therefore, the statement of the defendant, i.e., the husband, is accepted. And this is like the case where a person buys a horse and the seller has the option of three days. Whether he cancels the sale or retains it within three days, after the three days pass, the buyer says: You remained silent during the option period, and the seller says: I rejected (the sale). In this issue, the statement of the one who claims silence (the defendant) is accepted because silence is the original state and rejection is contrary. Similarly in the issue of the text, the statement of the one claiming silence, i.e., the husband, is accepted. Our argument is: The man claims that the marriage contract became binding and he became the owner of the sexual right (buda'), and the woman denies this. The original state is no marriage and no ownership of the buda'. Therefore, the man's statement is contrary to the original, and the woman's is in conformity. The one whose statement is contrary is the claimant, and the one whose statement conforms is the defendant. The claimant (man) does not have witnesses, so the statement of the defendant (woman) is accepted. And this is like the case where the depositary (muda') says: I have returned the deposit, and the owner says: You have not returned it. Here the depositary's statement is accepted because the depositary claims freedom of liability (faraagh dhimmah), and the owner claims liability. Freedom of liability is the original state, and liability is contrary. So the depositary is the defendant, and the owner is the claimant. The owner does not have evidence, so the defendant's statement is accepted. The response to Imam Zufar's analogy: In the case of the option condition, the sale contract became binding not because the statement of the one claiming silence was accepted, but because the contract became binding automatically upon the expiry of the option period. And in our issue, if the husband presents evidence of the woman's silence, the marriage is established because the claimant has supported his claim with proof. If he has no witnesses, then according to Imam (Abu Hanifa), the woman is not required to take an oath, and this is one of the six cases in which, according to Imam, the defendant is not sworn, unlike other scholars who hold that an oath is required. And God knows best what is correct.
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage addresses an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the probative value of silence in marriage claims when there are no witnesses. The scenario: a man claims marriage based on the woman's silence, while the woman claims she rejected. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, the woman's word is accepted without requiring her to take an oath. Imam Zufar opines that the man's word is accepted. The author begins by defining the claimant (mudda'i) as one whose statement contradicts the original state (asl), and the defendant (mudda'a alayh) as one whose statement conforms to it. He then references the principle from hadith that the burden of proof is on the claimant. In typical cases, if the claimant lacks evidence, the defendant's statement is accepted with an oath. However, Abu Hanifa exempts six specific cases from requiring the defendant's oath, and this marriage case is one of them. Zufar argues that silence is the original state (since it is non-speech), making the man the defendant, and thus his statement should be accepted. He draws an analogy to a sale with an option period: after the period passes, if the buyer claims the seller remained silent and the seller claims rejection, the buyer's (defendant's) statement is accepted. The Hanafi response refutes this analogy: in the sale case, the contract becomes binding automatically by the lapse of time, not by acceptance of silence. The Hanafi position bases itself on the principle that the original state is the absence of marriage, making the woman's denial in conformity, thus she is the defendant whose statement is accepted without oath. A further analogy is given: a depositary claiming return of a deposit is the defendant because freedom of liability is the original state, so his statement is accepted. The passage concludes by reaffirming that if the husband provides evidence, the marriage is established; otherwise, according to Abu Hanifa, the woman is not sworn, as one of the six exceptional cases.

Key Terms
مدعی (mudda'i): One whose statement is contrary to the original state (asl).
مدعی علیہ (mudda'a alayh): One whose statement conforms to the original state (asl).
بینہ (bayyinah): Evidence, specifically at least two male witnesses or one male and two females in most cases.
سکوت (sukut): Silence; in the context of marriage, it may or may not imply consent.
یمین (yamin): Oath taken by a defendant to support his statement.
استخلاف (istikhlaf): A set of six cases where defendant is not required to take an oath.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Because the original state is no marriage, so her denial conforms to the asl, making her the defendant.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi rule in marriage claims without witnesses: the woman's denial is accepted without oath because the default state is no marriage, making her the defendant.

Nikah — Marriage

The Guardian's Authority to Contract Marriage for Minors
Ruling allowing guardians (not just father/grandfather) to marry minors, with refutation of Malik and Shafi'i

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on which guardians can contract marriage for minors and evaluate the objections of Malik and Shafi'i using the school's dalil.

Core Idea
In Hanafi law, any agnatic relative can marry a minor to secure a suitable match.

In this passage, the Hidayah addresses a key dispute: who has authority to contract marriage for minor children? While Malik restricts to the father and Shafi'i to father and grandfather, the Hanafi school extends it to all asaba. We'll explore each position and see how the Hanafis support their view with qiyas and a hadith.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its scope
Read the matn: it states that marriage of a minor boy or girl is valid when contracted by the guardian, defined as any asaba relative. Note that the ruling applies whether the girl is virgin or non-virgin.
The guardian (wali) is the asaba, not just the father.
2
Locate the Hanafi dalil
The author cites the hadith: 'Marriage (authority) belongs to the asaba' and the order among asaba follows inheritance hierarchy. This is a primary proof for extending guardianship to all asaba.
Dalil is a Sunnah ahad, with zanni thubut and dalala.
3
Analyze the opposing schools' arguments
Malik argues that guardianship is based on need, and since minors lack desire, there is no need except for the father, whose authority is textually established. Shafi'i argues that only father and grandfather have sufficient affection; others cannot be trusted with such an important matter.
Both schools restrict guardianship to closer relatives.
4
Evaluate the Hanafi response
The Hanafis respond that need does exist: to secure a suitable match (kafa'ah). The grandfather is included by qiyas. For Shafi'i's point, they distinguish between validity of the contract and binding authority (wilayat al-ilzam): even if affection is deficient, the contract is still valid, and the deficiency only affects bindingness, not permissibility.
The Hanafi rebuttal uses qiyas and a distinction between validity and binding nature.
5
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah)
Under Hanafi law, an uncle or brother can marry a minor relative; under the other schools, only father (Malik) or father/grandfather (Shafi'i) can. Also, Shafi'i requires consent of a non-virgin minor, while Hanafi does not.
The difference affects who may act as guardian in real-life family situations.

Source Text
